Taking advantage of the economic opportunities of a two-state solution would require substantial investments from the public and private sectors of the international community and from both parties.) would pose significant threats to the socioeconomic welfare of Palestinians. This could further exacerbate the mental health crisis in Palestine.
1.2.2.5.5. Con: The health effects of Israeli occupation are largely attributable to [human rights violations](https://borgenproject.org/mental-health-in-palestine-the-hidden-plague/), not occupation per se. Given that occupation does not legitimize [human rights violations](https://guide-humanitarian-law.org/content/article/3/occupied-territory/)(Several recent decisions by international courts have also confirmed that the occupying power is obliged to comply with its human rights obligations in occupied territories and in respect of people placed under its effective control as a result of occupation or detention. These decisions thus confirm that the application of international humanitarian law is complementary to the conventions on human rights in these situations.), they can be mitigated regardless of the status of Palestinian statehood.
1.2.2.5.6. Pro: Israeli occupation makes it difficult for Palestinians to fulfill their basic needs for food, water, and shelter.
1.2.2.5.6.1. Pro: About one-third of Gaza's arable land and 85 percent of its fishing waters are totally or partially inaccessible due to [Israeli military measures](http://www.reuters.com/article/us-un-gaza-rights-idUSTRE78C59R20110913). As a result, at least two-thirds of Gazan households lack secure access to food.
1.2.2.5.6.1.1. Pro: Many Palestinians suffer from [malnutrition-related diseases](http://apps.who.int/gb/ebwha/pdf_files/WHA66/A66_INF1-en.pdf)(p. 11) due to a lack of access to food as a result of military barriers and movement restrictions.
1.2.2.5.6.1.2. Con: Food and resource scarcity is likely to have arisen because Hamas uses the [majority of the supplies](http://www.jewishvirtuallibrary.org/israel-s-quot-blockade-quot-of-gaza) brought into Gaza.
1.2.2.5.6.1.3. Pro: Blockades have left one million Palestinians in Gaza [without the ability](https://www.oxfam.org/en/timeline-humanitarian-impact-gaza-blockade)(The blockade has devastated Gaza’s economy, caused widespread destruction and left most people largely cut off from the outside world. Today, one million Palestinians in Gaza don't have enough food to feed their families, despite receiving food assistance or other forms of support.) to feed their family; this has a significant impact on the region.
1.2.2.5.6.2. Pro: Thousands of Palestinian homes and shops in East Jerusalem have been [destroyed](http://interactive.aljazeera.com/aje/2017/jerusalem-2016-home-demolitions/index.html) by the Israeli government.
1.2.2.5.6.3. Pro: In the West Bank, water resources have been seized allowing the Israeli settler population to consume approximately [three to eight times](https://www.alhaq.org/advocacy/18062.html) the amount of water used by the Palestinian population.
1.2.2.6. Pro: The occupation allows Israel to stifle and control the economy of the West Bank and Gaza.
1.2.2.6.1. Con: Israel-Palestine [trade](http://www.boi.org.il/he/NewsAndPublications/PressReleases/Documents/Israel-Palestinian%20trade.pdf) benefits the Palestinian economy significantly \(pp. 3, 11\).
1.2.2.6.1.1. Con: Since trade and investment are mutually beneficial, they would presumably continue even in the absence of occupation.
1.2.2.6.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.4.4.1.1.2.
1.2.2.6.1.3. Pro: [Palestinian purchases](http://www.boi.org.il/he/NewsAndPublications/PressReleases/Documents/Israel-Palestinian%20trade.pdf) and sales from and to Israel account for two-thirds and 81% of total Palestinian imports and exports, respectively \(p. 1\).
1.2.2.6.2. Pro: The professional and economic security of Palestinians is jeopardized because of Israeli occupation.
1.2.2.6.2.1. Con: There are more universities now than [before](https://unitedwithisrael.org/the-facts-show-israeli-occupation-is-good-for-the-palestinians/) the West Bank occupation.
1.2.2.6.2.1.1. Con: The UN, not Israel, provides the bulk of education in the West Bank and Gaza \([p. 3](http://www.ps.undp.org/content/dam/papp/docs/Publications/UNDP-papp-research-PHDR2015Education.pdf)\).
1.2.2.6.2.1.2. Con: The Palestine Technical-Tulkarem University College and the Al-Ummah Community College were established before 1967 \([p. 32](https://download.ei-ie.org/Docs/WebDepot/The%20Status%20of%20Higher%20Education%20Teaching%20Personnel%20in%20Israel,%20the%20West%20Bank%20and%20Gaza.pdf)\).
1.2.2.6.2.1.3. Pro: During the period of the British Mandate, the Peel Commission identified a lack of higher education opportunities for Palestinians as a major source of concern \([p. 31](https://download.ei-ie.org/Docs/WebDepot/The%20Status%20of%20Higher%20Education%20Teaching%20Personnel%20in%20Israel,%20the%20West%20Bank%20and%20Gaza.pdf)\).
1.2.2.6.2.1.4. Con: The number of universities in Palestine is not a good indicator of what Israel has done. These universities could have been created in spite of Israel, rather than because of Israel.
1.2.2.6.2.2. Pro: Israel's decision to [withhold taxes](https://download.ei-ie.org/Docs/WebDepot/The%20Status%20of%20Higher%20Education%20Teaching%20Personnel%20in%20Israel,%20the%20West%20Bank%20and%20Gaza.pdf)(\(p. 33\)) from the Palestinian Authority causes a severe lack of funding for schools.
1.2.2.6.3. Pro: Israel's control over Palestinian land use violates international regulations and deprives Palestinians of essential resources.
1.2.2.6.3.1. Con: Israel considers many of the [territories in question](https://theprint.in/theprint-essential/what-israel-palestine-conflict-is-all-about-and-why-both-want-west-bank-gaza-strip/323825/) to be its own. Thus, the distinction between "Israeli" use of "Palestinian" land is meaningless in the eyes of some Israelis.
1.2.2.6.3.2. Pro: 42% of the land in the West Bank has been [requisitioned](http://www.btselem.org/download/201007_by_hook_and_by_crook_eng.pdf)(pg.5) by Israel. This reduces the land available for use by the Palestinians.
1.2.2.6.3.2.1. Pro: Palestinian quarries in the West Bank have been [raided and requisitioned](https://www.btselem.org/planning_and_building/20160421_military_shuts_down_palestinian_quarries) by Israeli military forces.
1.2.2.6.3.2.2. Con: Palestinian quarries are mainly supported by Israeli customers, propping up a [vital industry](https://www.thenationalnews.com/world/the-palestinian-quarries-that-build-jerusalem-1.640799) that provides jobs and income for Palestinians.
1.2.2.6.3.2.3. Pro: Palestinians' land is [confiscated](http://www.aljazeera.com/indepth/features/2017/02/palestinians-decry-west-bank-industrial-zone-expansion-170206090749576.html) without compensation in order to allow construction.
1.2.2.6.3.3. Pro: Israel’s confiscation of land, water, and other natural resources for the benefit of settlements and residents of Israel also violate the [Hague Regulations of 1907](http://www.eccpalestine.org/israels-obligations-as-an-occupying-power-under-international-law-its-violations-and-implications-for-eu-policy/), which prohibit an occupying power from expropriating the resources of occupied territory for its own benefit.
1.2.2.6.3.4. Pro: Israel [prohibits](https://www.btselem.org/download/201105_dispossession_and_exploitation_eng.pdf)(pg.31) Palestinians from using over three-quarters of the Jordan Valley and northern Dead Sea area. On the remaining land, Israel limits Palestinian building and prevents any possibility of sustainable development of Palestinian communities.
1.2.2.6.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.2.4.6.3.
1.2.2.6.5. Con: For many Palestinians, Israeli jobs are the [only](http://www.reuters.com/article/us-israel-palestinians-workers-idUSKCN0VV1J6) ones available to them. They provide much needed employment and considerably higher wages than Palestinian jobs.
1.2.2.6.5.1. Pro: Security officials in Israel have advocated for employing more Palestinians in Israel, as increased economic activity [reduces](http://www.timesofisrael.com/israel-said-to-okay-work-permits-for-30000-more-palestinians/) the appeal of terrorism. As a result, the Israeli government is planning to [increase](http://www.haaretz.com/israel-news/.premium-1.759885) the number of work permits given to Palestinians.
1.2.2.6.5.1.1. Con: Work permits are [distributed arbitrarily](http://www.aljazeera.com/news/2015/07/permit-laws-west-bank-workers-israel-150729094809544.html); young, unmarried Palestinian men rarely receive one as they are deemed to be a security threat. This prevents fully employment in Palestine.
1.2.2.6.5.1.2. Pro: Within a span of four years \(2011-2015\), the number of Palestinians working in Israel [doubled](http://www.haaretz.com/israel-news/business/.premium-1.645266). This underscores the benefits of increasing Israeli businesses and Palestinian workers for both parties.
1.2.2.6.5.2. Pro: Israeli businesses within settlements [provide tens of thousands of Palestinians with jobs](http://www.reuters.com/article/us-israel-palestinians-workers-idUSKCN0VV1J6) and a source of income.
1.2.2.6.5.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.2.2.6.5.1.2.
1.2.2.6.5.2.2. Con: Giving someone a job does not equate to them having a high quality of life or even a minimally enjoyable life.
1.2.2.6.5.2.3. Pro: [Working in Palestine](https://www.jpost.com/israel-news/palestinian-workers-in-israel-seizing-opportunity-or-being-exploited-675338) makes more economic sense not only for Palestinians but also the West Bank, as the minimum wage in Israel is double of what it is in West Bank.
1.2.2.6.5.2.4. Con: Palestinian laborers who work in Israeli settlements are [exploited](https://www.btselem.org/download/201105_dispossession_and_exploitation_eng.pdf)(pg.37) and paid as little as one-third the minimum wage, while their employers consistently violate safety regulations.
1.2.2.6.5.3. Con: Palestinian workers experience a [lack of oversight](https://www.hrw.org/news/2016/01/19/occupation-inc-how-settlement-businesses-contribute-israels-violations-palestinian) on labour and environmental regulations than their Israeli counterparts, and no additional benefits. One can hardly call them a gift to the Palestinian people.
1.2.2.6.5.4. Con: Israel's preferential treatment towards Israeli businesses forces Palestinians to depend on them, while Palestinian businesses are prevented from growing.
1.2.2.6.5.4.1. Pro: Israel [provides](https://www.hrw.org/news/2016/01/19/occupation-inc-how-settlement-businesses-contribute-israels-violations-palestinian) financial and regulatory incentives and permits to Israeli businesses for the construction and operation of settlement companies while severely restricting such permits and incentives for Palestinian businesses.
1.2.2.6.5.4.1.1. Pro: Only [2%](https://interactive.aljazeera.com/aje/2017/jerusalem-2016-home-demolitions/index.html) of Palestinian construction permit requests are approved.
1.2.2.6.5.4.2. Con: Every country provides preferential treatment for their own businesses; protecting domestic enterprise is a common practice.
1.2.2.6.5.4.3. Pro: Since 1994, the military has been [systematically refusing](https://www.hrw.org/news/2016/04/21/israel-quarry-shutdown-harms-palestinians) to issue permits for Palestinian quarries, while allocating large swaths of land in the West Bank for quarries in Israeli settlements. This costs the Palestinian economy at least US$ 241 million per year.
1.2.2.6.5.5. Con: The World Bank [found](http://www.worldbank.org/en/news/feature/2010/09/21/the-underpinnings-of-the-future-palestinian-state-sustainable-growth-and-institutions) that Israel preventing access to the majority of the land and water resources in the occupied territories to Palestinians was the cause of underdevelopment in Palestine.
1.2.3. Con: There is little reason to believe that a change in a diplomatic policy would result in a corresponding change in leadership style from anyone involved in the conflict. As such, the conflict has been classified as [intractable](https://www.huffpost.com/entry/the-mathematics-of-middle_b_887723)(The Israeli-Palestinian conflict is an immensely complicated hundred-year-old conflict that today operates and is reinforced across a multitude of issues, time periods, stakeholders and lands. It has become what Stephen Cohen, founder of the Institute for Middle East Peace and Development describes as “the crucible of multiple conflicts in the region and multiple grievances that feed upon one another and that produce reoccurring eruptions of violence.”).
1.2.3.1. Pro: As the Palestinian leadership \(both [Hamas](https://www.timesofisrael.com/hamas-assures-critics-israels-destruction-still-a-goal/) and [Fatah](https://www.timesofisrael.com/hamas-assures-critics-israels-destruction-still-a-goal/)\) has been clear that an independent Palestine will not end the conflict, recognition of Palestine would simply reward Palestinian intransigence.
1.2.3.1.1. Pro: [93% of Palestinians](https://global100.adl.org/country/west-bank-and-gaza/2014) hold anti-Semitic views; an independent Palestine would not alter these so conflict would likely continue.
1.2.3.2. Con: The Palestinian Authority is forced to be undemocratic in order to ensure some semblance of stability. This may change after recognition.
1.2.3.2.1. Pro: Palestinians are better off independent and undemocratic than undemocratic and subservient to Israel, as many of the [difficulties](https://www.hrw.org/report/2021/04/27/threshold-crossed/israeli-authorities-and-crimes-apartheid-and-persecution) faced by Palestinians are imposed by them.
1.2.3.2.1.1. Pro: Israel has [halted](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/jan/03/israel-tax-payments-withhold-palestine-international-criminal-court) transfers of the tax revenue it collects on behalf of the Palestinians in retaliation for their move to join the International Criminal Court. This has exacerbated economic woes that Fatah cannot abate.
1.2.3.2.2. Con: Corruption and patronage politics is [widespread](http://www.thetower.org/3414-new-survey-96-of-palestinians-believe-palestinian-authority-is-corrupt/) and [endemic](https://al-shabaka.org/briefs/corruption-in-palestine/)(As many as 81% of the Palestinians living in the occupied Palestinian territory believe there is corruption in Palestinian Authority institutions according to a recent survey, perceptions reinforced by the recently launched annual report of the Palestinian Coalition for Accountability and Integrity \(AMAN\), the Transparency International chapter in Palestine. These perceptions persist despite former Prime Minister Salam Fayyad’s much-touted state-building efforts to root out corruption - and are at variance with international reports finding that suggest improvement in good governance.) in the Palestinian Authority; this may be very difficult to remediate regardless of the recognition of Palestine.
1.2.3.2.2.1. Pro: The Palestinian Authority Security Forces \(PASF\) are beset by violent [patronage-based](http://eprints.lse.ac.uk/64699/1/The%20evolution%20and%20reform%20of%20Palestinian.pdf)(p. 6) behaviour. This could lead to [infighting and divisions](http://www.nytimes.com/2013/06/27/opinion/global/the-bane-of-palestinian-infighting.html) in the security forces, if an independent Palestinian state is created.
1.2.3.2.2.2. Pro: In the first Palestinian audit conducted in 1997, nearly 40% of the Palestinian Authority budget had been [misappropriated](http://www.u4.no/publications/overview-of-corruption-and-anti-corruption-in-palestine/). A 2011 report by [Transparency Palestine](https://www.aman-palestine.org/en/reports-and-studies/1387.html) indicated a continuation of this trend.
1.2.3.2.3. Con: President Mahmoud Abbas is serving his 16th year of a supposed four-year term. He has [not called for elections](http://www.washingtontimes.com/news/2016/dec/6/mahmoud-abbas-no-champion-of-democracy-for-palesti/) and instead chooses to rule by presidential decree.
1.2.3.2.3.1. Pro: Abbas has not [openly cultivated](http://www.worldpoliticsreview.com/trend-lines/15857/abbas-stumhttps://mosaicmagazine.com/picks/2017/02/mahmoud-abbass-appointment-of-a-new-deputy-chairman-wont-prevent-a-violent-succession-struggle/bles-at-home-despite-palestine-recognition-gains-abroad) a successor which may lead to a violent succession struggle and worse governance.
1.2.3.2.3.2. Con: Leaders of [other countries](https://www.arabnews.com/node/1689281) have established themselves as leaders for life, with no threats to the recognition of their states.
1.2.3.2.3.3. Pro: Most Palestinians would like Abbas to [resign](https://apnews.com/article/middle-east-jerusalem-israel-mahmoud-abbas-hamas-5a716da863a603ab5f117548ea85379d).
1.2.3.2.4. Con: The Palestinian Authority [engages in brutal suppression](https://www.opendemocracy.net/en/north-africa-west-asia/killing-nizar-banat-and-authoritarianism-palestine/) of Palestinians in order to maintain its power. This does not contribute to stability.
1.2.3.2.4.1. Pro: The Palestinian Authority is increasingly moving towards establishing a [police state](https://www.middleeastmonitor.com/20160524-a-palestinian-police-state-in-waiting/) as has been [violently](https://www.amnesty.org/en/latest/news/2017/03/evidence-shows-palestinian-security-forces-violently-suppressed-peaceful-protest-in-ramallah/) and brutally suppressing peaceful protests through use of [excessive force](https://www.amnesty.org/en/documents/mde21/006/2013/en/) for many years.
1.2.3.2.4.2. Pro: The PA’s [Preventive Security and General Intelligence](https://www.hrw.org/report/2011/04/06/no-news-good-news/abuses-against-journalists-palestinian-security-forces) security agencies violate the basic rights of journalists and limit their ability to perform their reporting duties. This is aimed at censoring free speech and minimizing reports of their own abuses.
1.2.3.2.5. Pro: Many countries are [undemocratic dictatorships](https://borgenproject.org/top-five-non-democratic-countries/). Non-adherence to democratic values neither warrants nor justifies the denial of recognition of statehood.
1.2.3.3. Pro: US recognition of Palestine does not force Israel to do anything; it is plausible that Israel would maintain the status quo and leadership style, and the harms would persist.
1.2.3.3.1. Pro: The American political establishment effectively filters out the candidates that do not conform to certain [attitudes](https://www.nytimes.com/2014/08/03/opinion/sunday/roger-cohen-why-americans-see-israel-the-way-they-do.html?_r=0)(In the United States, by contrast, support for Israel remains strong \(although less so among the young, who are most exposed to the warring hashtags of social media\). That support is overwhelming in political circles. Palestinian suffering remains near taboo in Congress. It is not only among American Jews, better organized and more outspoken than their whispering European counterparts, that the story of a nation of immigrants escaping persecution and rising from nowhere in the Holy Land resonates. The Israeli saga — of courage and will — echoes in American mythology, far beyond religious identification, be it Jewish or evangelical Christian.) towards Israel and Palestine.
1.2.3.3.2. Pro: Israel's [Netanyahu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Netanyahu) served three terms as Prime Minister. The fact that Israelis consistently voted for him shows that they approved of his approach to Palestine.
1.2.3.3.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.4.1.3.
1.2.3.3.2.2. Con: Netanyahu is on [trial for corruption](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2021/nov/22/former-netanyahu-aide-testifies-corruption-trial-nir-hefetz), in part for giving preferential treatment to a company in exchange for positive reviews in the media. His firm grip over his public image could have convinced people he was doing a better job than he was in reality.
1.2.3.3.3. Con: Respecting and recognizing a people's right to self-determination should not depend upon whether it serves other political goals, such as the resolution of the conflict between Israel and Palestine.
1.2.3.3.3.1. Con: Recognizing Palestine as a state may lead to the empowerment Hamas, which rules over the Gaza strip and is a US-designated terrorist organization. The right to self-determination of a terrorist organization should not be valued above the safety of Palestinian population and Palestine's regional neighbors.
1.2.3.3.3.2. Pro: The right to self-determination of people is a founding principle of the UN charter \([p. 3](https://treaties.un.org/doc/publication/ctc/uncharter.pdf)\).
1.2.4. Pro: [Israeli settlements](http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-middle-east-38888649) would be tantamount to violating the sovereignty of Palestine if the latter was recognised as a state. This makes settlements less likely to expand, and more likely to be dismantled.
1.2.4.1. Pro: Palestine is currently [too weak](https://www.trtworld.com/magazine/this-is-the-imbalance-of-power-between-israel-and-palestine-in-real-terms-46651)(The Israeli Defence Forces \(IDF\) has nearly 170,000 active military personnel while it lists more than three million males and females available for army service. \[...\] On the other hand, without any real state or regular army, different Palestinian resistance groups have much less manpower than the Israeli army, amounting to circa 30,000 to 50,000 in troops.) to prevent settler expansion militarily, and as such the US should intervene to mitigate this on their behalf.
1.2.4.1.1. Con: Even if no new settlements are built, it is hard to imagine how the [800,000 Jewish settlers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_statistics_for_Israeli_settlements_in_the_West_Bank) already living on Palestinian land could be removed.
1.2.4.1.1.1. Con: Israelis may imagine being rounded up, herded into prisons, and systematically executed.
1.2.4.1.1.1.1. Pro: This is evident in their reaction to even the slightest criticism of their government or its policies, which is often fear-based, leading to [accusations of antisemitism](https://www.nif.org/blog/israels-abuse-and-misuse-of-the-word-anti-semitism/).
1.2.4.1.1.1.2. Pro: Israelis are [taught about the Holocaust](https://www.jstor.org/stable/43057186).
1.2.4.1.2. Pro: Israel recently [authorised](http://www.aljazeera.com/news/2017/02/israel-authorises-3000-settler-homes-west-bank-170201051854191.html) 3,000 more settler homes in the West Bank. These will likely continue unless the Palestinian state is established.
1.2.4.2. Con: Despite being [illegal](https://www.un.org/press/en/2016/sc12657.doc.htm) Israeli settlements have not stopped expanding. Even if the US recognises Palestine, the illegality will not be significantly different to the status quo.
1.2.4.3. Pro: According to the [UN Security Council](https://www.un.org/press/en/2016/sc12657.doc.htm), Israeli settlements in Palestinian territories occupied since 1967 have no legal validity and constitute a flagrant violation of international law. Thus, dismantling them is justified.
1.2.5. Con: Recognizing Palestine could put historic monuments and antiquities at risk of destruction from violence.
1.2.5.1. Pro: Jewish temples [existed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herodium#Synagogue) in the [West](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamla#Synagogue) [Bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Masada#Yigael_Yadin_expedition) from around 70 AD. Withdrawal from Gaza has lead to the [destruction](https://www.latimes.com/archives/la-xpm-2005-sep-12-fg-gaza12-story.html) of Jewish temples. If history repeats itself, withdrawal from the West Bank could lead to similar destruction of many Roman Era Jewish temples.
1.2.5.2. Pro: An independent Palestinian authority in the West Bank could put antiquities at risk as they [lack the capacity](https://apnews.com/article/egypt-ap-top-news-international-news-science-militant-groups-03b997d4565b4db799c6d0fe4a1fc263) to care for the sites.
1.2.5.3. Con: The transition from US recognition of Palestine to an independent Palestine would likely be incredibly slow, allowing time for protective infrastructure to be built around important historical sites.
1.2.5.4. Con: It is not clear why Palestinians would destroy historic monuments as they likely have similar attachments to these sites as Israelis.
1.3. Pro: Palestine is a legitimate state and deserves to be recognised as such.
1.3.1. Pro: The legal definition of a state is enshrined in international law via the [Montevideo Convention of Statehood](https://www.ilsa.org/Jessup/Jessup15/Montevideo%20Convention.pdf) \(p. 3\); Palestine fulfills the criteria outlined in the convention.
1.3.1.1. Con: Palestine lacks a single sovereign government which can enforce laws throughout its dominion.
1.3.1.1.1. Pro: Palestine is [politically divided](https://www.brookings.edu/blog/markaz/2015/09/21/hamas-or-the-palestinian-authority-who-will-fail-first/) between two factions: Hamas, which controls the Gaza Strip, and Fatah which controls the West Bank.
1.3.1.1.2. Con: Even though Abbas's government does not have control over the entire territory, it is already the only sovereign recognized by most world governments, including the United Nations, whose [representative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestinian_Ambassador_to_the_United_Nations) is appointed by Abbas. In this respect, it is no different than any other state that does not fully control its territory.
1.3.1.1.3. Con: [Pakistan](http://foreignpolicy.com/2014/08/26/militancy-in-pakistan/), [Nigeria](http://studies.aljazeera.net/en/reports/2016/06/resurgence-militancy-nigerias-oil-rich-niger-delta-dangers-militarisation-160608065729726.html), [India](http://www.thestatesman.com/opinion/islamic-militancy-in-india-1478730250.html), [Ukraine](http://www.aljazeera.com/indepth/interactive/2017/02/ukraine-map-170205081953296.html), and [Phillippines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrorism_in_the_Philippines) are just a few of the many states in the world that have militant organisations carrying out violent atrocities within their borders, yet they are all recognised as states.
1.3.1.2. Con: Palestine [does not have contiguous borders](http://america.aljazeera.com/multimedia/2014/7/west-bank-security.html), but is instead made up of an archipelago of areas of control, broken up by Israeli settlements and highways.
1.3.1.2.1. Con: It is unclear why the nature of a state's borders bears on its legitimacy, particularly in a modern context in which physical travel is less important.
1.3.1.2.2. Con: It is circular to deny Palestine the recognition of a state, which would presumably consolidate its territory, because its territory is not consolidated at the moment.
1.3.1.2.3. Con: Other countries, too, have split territories and exclaves. [French Guiana](https://www.britannica.com/place/French-Guiana) is part of France, [Alaska](https://theconversation.com/why-russia-gave-up-alaska-americas-gateway-to-the-arctic-74675) and [Hawaii](https://restoftheiceberg.org/posts/2017/10/24/why-is-hawaii-part-of-the-usa) are part of the United States and the [Kaliningrad Oblast](https://www.theguardian.com/cities/2018/may/31/kaliningrad-the-russian-enclave-with-a-taste-for-europe) is part of Russia, even though none of them share a border with their country's main territory.
1.3.1.2.4. Pro: Netanyahu proposed a plan to [apply sovereignty](https://www.bbc.com/news/world-middle-east-52756427) on Jewish settlement in the West Bank, effectively annexing 30% of the West Bank and creating a multitude of Palestinian enclaves.
1.3.1.3. Pro: Palestine has the "capacity to enter into relations with the other states" as it has a web of [diplomatic representations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_diplomatic_missions_of_Palestine) around the world.
1.3.1.4. Pro: Palestine clearly fulfills the [territory](https://www.aljazeera.com/news/2020/6/26/palestine-and-israel-mapping-an-annexation) criteria as it rests upon a strip on the West Bank and in Gaza.
1.3.1.4.1. Con: The territory of the proposed Palestinian state is disputed. More than half of the West Bank, Area C, has a [majority Jewish population](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Area_C_\(West_Bank\)) that would rather stay under Israel's sovereignty.
1.3.1.4.1.1. Con: A significant portion of the Jewish population of Area C lives there due to [illegal settlement](https://academic.oup.com/icon/article/4/3/540/646388)(The real controversy hovering over all the litigation on the security barrier concerns the fate of the Israeli settlements in the occupied territories. Since 1967, Israel has allowed and even encouraged its citizens to live in the new settlements established in the territories, motivated by religious and national sentiments attached to the history of the Jewish nation in the land of Israel. This policy has also been justified in terms of security interests, taking into consideration the dangerous geographic circumstances of Israel before 1967 \(where Israeli areas on the Mediterranean coast were potentially threatened by Jordanian control of the West Bank ridge\). The international community, for its part, has viewed this policy as patently illegal, based on the provisions of the Fourth Geneva Convention that prohibit moving populations to or from territories under occupation. \(pg.548\)).
1.3.1.4.2. Pro: Whilst there have been significant settler attempts to [undermine](https://www.aljazeera.com/news/2021/7/8/how-israel-backs-settlers-to-confiscate-palestinian-lands)(In early May, more than 50 Jewish families packed their bags and moved to a hilltop in the West Bank in the occupied Palestinian territory.\n\n...\nThe land is also strategically located. It lies south of Nablus in an area called Jabal Sabih in the villages of Beita and Yatma that is expected to be a part of the future state of Palestine. A settlement here would break Palestinian territorial contiguity.\n\nLast week, the settlers were finally evicted and the Palestinians celebrated it as a victory of their resistance.) Palestinian contiguity, some have recently been overruled.
1.3.1.4.2.1. Con: The Israeli government, under Naftali Bennett, is expected to use an [old Ottoman law](https://www.aljazeera.com/news/2021/7/8/how-israel-backs-settlers-to-confiscate-palestinian-lands)(The Israeli media reported Naftali Bennett, Israel’s new prime minister and a staunch supporter of illegal settlements, offered the settlers a deal – the state would ascertain whether the land can be classified as “state land” and if yes – the conclusion the state is expected to arrive at – it would be handed over to the settlers even though it lies in Palestinian villages.\n\nHagit Ofran, executive director of the Settlement Watch programme at the Israeli NGO Peace Now, surmised it for Al Jazeera. “It was published: The settlers leave; the houses remain; the army puts up a military post; the government starts the process of declaring state lands,” Ofran said.\n\nIsrael legalises such outposts or settlements by deploying a draconian interpretation of the Ottoman law that if the land was not cultivated for several years in a row it would become the property of the state.) to claim the land.
1.3.1.4.2.1.1. Pro: If attempts to weaponise old Ottoman land law to legitimise settlements go [unchallenged](https://www.aljazeera.com/news/2021/7/8/how-israel-backs-settlers-to-confiscate-palestinian-lands)(Will Biden pull him up and demand a settlement freeze, or will he just give him a slap on the wrist and agree to disagree?\n\nOfran of Peace Now said if left unchallenged by the United States, Bennet would of course want to continue with “the legalisation” of all outposts.\n\n“The question would be to what extent the pressure from within the government and from the world will lead him to refrain from it,” Ofran said.) by Biden, then they are likely to become a mainstay of land claims from Bennett's government.
1.3.1.4.2.2. Pro: It seems unlikely that recently attempts to claim land that settlers have been expelled from will be successful, as it was [developed](https://www.aljazeera.com/news/2021/7/8/how-israel-backs-settlers-to-confiscate-palestinian-lands)(Israel legalises such outposts or settlements by deploying a draconian interpretation of the Ottoman law that if the land was not cultivated for several years in a row it would become the property of the state.\n\nPeace Now, however, asserted the Evyatar outpost was in fact built on “private Palestinian land”, and that an aerial photo from 1980 showed parts of the land were even “cultivated” – implying it cannot hence be deemed state land.) by Palestinians in recent history.
1.3.1.5. Pro: Palestine has a permanent population of around [4.7 million](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Demographics_of_the_Palestinian_territories). Therefore, Palestine fulfills [criterion 1\(a\)](https://www.ilsa.org/Jessup/Jessup15/Montevideo%20Convention.pdf) of the Montevideo Convention \(p. 3\).
1.3.1.6. Con: Palestine does not have a [monopoly on violence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monopoly_on_violence) over the area it claims.
1.3.1.6.1. Con: Recognition of statehood from the US could increase the ability of Palestinian governments to exert power within their borders and establish a monopoly on violence.
1.3.1.6.2. Pro: Palestine currently has no unitary security force. Fatah [controls](https://themedialine.org/by-region/backgrounder-palestinian-security-services-explained/) the Palestinian Security Services, while Hamas has [de-facto created](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestinian_Security_Services#The_Executive_Force) its own security force in the Gaza Strip.
1.3.1.6.3. Pro: The cause of Palestine's failure to meet traditional concepts of statehood is Israeli occupation.
1.3.1.6.3.1. Pro: Israeli forces [regularly conduct raids](https://www.amnesty.org/en/location/middle-east-and-north-africa/israel-and-occupied-palestinian-territories/report-israel-and-occupied-palestinian-territories/) into Palestinian areas of the West Bank to arrest suspected terrorists.
1.3.1.6.3.1.1. Con: Israeli intrusion should not render a state illegitimate: the US frequently intervenes militarily in countries without their consent, but that does not make their governments illegitimate.
1.3.1.6.3.1.2. Con: The frequency of these raids would presumably diminish drastically if this recognition strengthened the ability of the Palestinian state to maintain control itself.
1.3.1.6.3.2. Pro: Official recognition of statehood by the US could facilitate processes to unify control of Palestine; the granting of statehood could be made contingent on this happening.
1.3.1.6.3.2.1. Pro: Fatah and Hamas have [already indicated](http://www.haaretz.com/israel-news/1.765719) a willingness to form a unity government. US recognition could enable greater international cooperation in the formation of that government.
1.3.1.6.3.3. Con: Israeli occupation is unlikely to change, should the USA recognise Palestine as a state.
1.3.2. Pro: Recognition by many other nations confirms a state's legitimacy as it [normalises](https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/legitimacy/)(As is well known, Weber distinguishes among three main sources of legitimacy—understood as the acceptance both of authority and of the need to obey its commands. People may have faith in a particular political or social order because it has been there for a long time \(tradition\), because they have faith in the rulers \(charisma\), or because they trust its legality—specifically the rationality of the rule of law.) the social order of the state, and brings it into the sphere of international relations and law.
1.3.2.1. Pro: Many other states have recognised, or indicated their willingness to recognise, an independent Palestinian state - this indicates that it has an appropriate status as a nation.
1.3.2.1.1. Pro: One-hundred thirty-five members of the UN [recognise](https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/worldviews/wp/2014/11/07/map-the-countries-that-recognize-palestine-as-a-state/?utm_term=.75a6e0dbe01e) Palestine as a state. Palestine was therefore recognised by a [higher percentage of nations](http://www.huffingtonpost.com/dashanne-stokes/is-this-the-real-reason-why-us-wont-recognize-palestine_b_9777246.html) at the time of its failed application to the U.N. in 2011 \(68 percent\) than Israel was \(64 percent\) when it was successfully admitted in 1949.
1.3.2.1.1.1. Pro: Palestine has [more states recognising it](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_states_with_limited_recognition) than any other partially-recognised state not recognised by the US.
1.3.2.1.2. Pro: The European Union has consistently declared its support and readiness for recognising a Palestinian state. This is outlined in both the 1980 Venice Declaration and [Berlin Declaration of March 1999](http://www.jcpa.org/jl/vp418.htm) \([p. 1](https://eeas.europa.eu/archives/docs/mepp/docs/venice_declaration_1980_en.pdf)\).
1.3.2.1.2.1. Pro: The EU has [invested heavily](http://www.ecfr.eu/article/commentary_five_reasons_to_recognise_a_palestinian_state360) in the two-state solution; since 2007, EU member states have provided almost 2.5 billion euros worth of support to Palestinian state building efforts, not including annual contributions of more than 200 million euros for the UNRWA, the UN Relief Work Agency that carries out projects in the occupied territories.
1.3.2.2. Pro: The people of Palestine have the right to self-determination - this is far easier to attain when their state is internationally recognised.
1.3.2.2.1. Con: Palestinians have a [right to a nationality](http://www.ohchr.org/EN/Issues/Pages/Nationality.aspx); however, this does not imply a UN right to a state of their own. Their rights to security and a stable life could be equally satisfied by giving them citizenship in another country, either Israel or Jordan.
1.3.2.2.1.1. Con: Jordan has [no interest](https://www.gatestoneinstitute.org/4615/jordan-palestinians) in accepting Palestinians as its own citizens, in part because it fears being transformed into a Palestinian state. It currently refuses entry and citizenship to, or forcibly deports, Palestinian refugees.
1.3.2.2.1.1.1. Con: [Millions of Palestinians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestinians_in_Jordan) already have, or are eligible to apply for, Jordanian citizenship.
1.3.2.2.1.1.2. Pro: Jordan has recently been [arbitrarily withdrawing citizenship](https://www.hrw.org/report/2010/02/01/stateless-again/palestinian-origin-jordanians-deprived-their-nationality) from people of Palestinian origin.
1.3.2.2.1.1.3. Pro: The [easiest path to citizenship](https://www.unrwa.org/content/jordanian-nationality) is only open to Palestinians that lived in Jordan between December 1949 and February 1954.
1.3.2.2.1.1.4. Pro: Following the loss of the West Bank to Israel, Jordan became actively hostile against Palestinians in the northwest of the country; terminating in a conflict which suppressed Palestinians, expelled militants, and [permanently scarred relations](https://minorityrights.org/minorities/palestinians-2/) between the two nations.
1.3.2.2.1.1.5. Pro: Palestinians living in Jordan but originally from the Gaza Strip, which was under Egyptian authority until 1967, have [no right to Jordanian citizenship](https://www.justice.gov/sites/default/files/eoir/legacy/2013/12/04/JOR35401-Palestinians-West%20Bank.pdf)(p. 1).
1.3.2.2.2. Pro: As Palestinians were [forcibly expelled](https://www.vox.com/2018/11/20/18080030/israel-palestine-nakba) from Israel, their claim to sovereignty was never legitimately extinguished, so they still have a right to control over parts of the region.
1.3.2.2.2.1. Con: The current nonexistence of a Palestinian state is [due to](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_of_Palestine) the rejection of a partition plan by Arab leaders in 1948 and subsequent acts of warfare against the new state of Israel by Arab nations. Given that Palestinians voluntarily forfeited their opportunity to receive a state, they are not entitled to one now.
1.3.2.2.2.1.1. Con: The U.N. partition [recommendation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Partition_Plan_for_Palestine) called for more than half of then Palestinian land to be allocated for a “Jewish State”. This was unreasonable as the Arabs were a majority in Palestine at the time, and owned more land than Jews in every single district in the region. The 1948 plan was unfair and deserved to be rejected.
1.3.2.2.2.1.1.1. Con: Even if the agreement was unfair, the [violent reaction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Partition_Plan_for_Palestine#Arab_states)(A few weeks after UNSCOP released its report, Azzam Pasha, the General Secretary of the Arab League, told an Egyptian newspaper "Personally I hope the Jews do not force us into this war because it will be a war of elimination and it will be a dangerous massacre which history will record similarly to the Mongol massacre or the wars of the Crusades." \[...\] Azzam told Alec Kirkbride "We will sweep them \[the Jews\] into the sea." Syrian president Shukri al-Quwatli told his people: "We shall eradicate Zionism.") against Israel from surrounding Arab nations was unjustified.
1.3.2.2.2.1.1.1.1. Con: Violent reactions can be justifiable; the [German occupation of Poland](https://www.spiegel.de/international/europe/germany-s-wwii-occupation-of-poland-when-we-finish-nobody-is-left-alive-a-759095.html) was unfair, and the subsequent violent reaction against Germany was justifiable.
1.3.2.2.2.1.2. Pro: Palestine has [no history](https://theconversation.com/the-history-of-israel-and-palestine-alternative-names-competing-claims-163156)(At that time, the land comprised three administrative regions of the Ottoman empire, none of which was called “Palestine.”\n\nIn 1917, the land came under British rule. In 1923, “Mandatory Palestine,” which also included the current state of Jordan, was created. Its Arab inhabitants saw themselves primarily not as “Palestinians” in the sense of a nation, but instead as Arabs living in Palestine \(or rather, “Greater Syria”\).) as a unified state; it existed as part of other states before the 20th century.
1.3.2.2.2.1.2.1. Pro: [Arab leaders in 1919](https://www.jewishvirtuallibrary.org/jsource/images/mf2017.pdf#page=10)(Prior to partition, Palestinian Arabs did not view themselves as having a separate identity. When the First Congress of Muslim-Christian Associations met in Jerusalem in February 1919 to choose Palestinian representatives for the Paris Peace Conference, they adopted the fol-lowing resolution: We consider Palestine as part of Arab Syria, as it has never been separated from it at any time. We are connected with it by national, religious, linguistic, natural, economic, and geo-graphical bonds. Similarly, the King-Crane commission found that Christian and Muslim Arabs opposed any plan to create a country called “Palestine,” because it was viewed as recognition of Zionist claims. In 1937, a local Arab leader, Auni Bey Abdul Hadi, told the Peel Commission, which ultimately suggested the partition of Palestine: “There is no such country as Palestine! ‘Palestine’ is a term the Zionists invented! There is no Palestine in the Bible. Our country was for centuries part of Syria.” \(pg.3\)) denied that there is a distinct country called Palestine, instead associating it with Syria.
1.3.2.2.2.1.2.2. Con: Whilst Palestine was not a state historically, it is considered one today as those living within it [request statehood](https://www.cfr.org/backgrounder/palestinian-statehood-un), and unified recognition.
1.3.2.2.2.1.2.3. Pro: Given that there are no historical roots for the state of Palestine, the US should not recognize them; instead, the US should recognize the Palestinians as a distinct people living within the state of Israel.
1.3.2.2.2.2. Con: Palestinians never had a claim to sovereignty over the area in the first place; [Britain did](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine), as Palestine prior to 1948 was part of the British Mandate.
1.3.2.2.2.2.1. Con: The [British Mandate for Palestine](https://unispal.un.org/UNISPAL.NSF/0/2FCA2C68106F11AB05256BCF007BF3CB) was a piece of legislation produced by a colonial organisation, [the League of Nations](https://brill.com/view/journals/iclr/22/3-4/article-p275_2.xml?language=en#FN000050), with little regard for the rights of Palestinian Arabs. It should not be considered a legitimate cession of Palestinian moral ownership of the area.
1.3.2.2.2.2.2. Pro: The [British Mandate](https://encyclopedia.1914-1918-online.net/article/british_mandate_for_palestine) was a legally binding instrument created by the League of Nations and backed by the San Remo declaration and the treaty of Sevres \(1920\) whereby the previous sovereign divested itself of any claim to the area. The mandate recognised the rights of Jews to reconstitute their national home.
1.3.2.2.2.3. Pro: [Historical records](https://www.afsc.org/resource/palestinian-refugees-and-right-return) disprove the theory that Palestinian Arabs fled the state voluntarily or at the behest of Arab leaders. Instead, they were forced to flee because their lives were endangered.
1.3.2.2.3. Pro: Palestinians previously inhabited an area greater than it currently does - much of their land has been [stolen](http://www.slate.com/articles/news_and_politics/the_gist/1997/05/selling_land_to_jews.html), actively restricting their right to self-determination.
1.3.2.2.3.1. Pro: The conquest of Palestinian territories by Israel in the [Six-Day War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Six-Day_War) was illegitimate.
1.3.2.2.3.1.1. Con: As the land taken in the defensive war did not belong to any entity called "Palestine" framing the claim as a "conquest of Palestinian territories" is fallacious. The area in question had been held by Jordan.
1.3.2.2.3.2. Con: While individual Palestinians may have claims to land because it was stolen from them, this doesn't establish the legitimacy of a Palestinian state that postdates this theft.
1.3.2.2.3.2.1. Con: All Palestinians have suffered from the flow-on consequences of the initial theft of land; they therefore all have a moral claim to the land.
1.3.2.2.3.2.2. Con: At the very least, by indicating that the Israeli claim to the land is based on theft and therefore not legitimate, it strengthens the Palestinian claim to the land and therefore to self-determination.
1.3.2.2.3.2.3. Con: If modern Israel's legitimacy postdates its historical narrative, which is over 2,000 years old, then the 100-year-old Palestinian rights are also valid.
1.3.2.2.3.3. Pro: Expulsions and the illegal occupation of land that had been temporarily vacated during the war of 1948, called [the Palestinian Exodus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1948_Palestinian_exodus), shaped the land claim of the early Israeli state.
1.3.2.2.3.4. Con: Jews existed in British-mandated Palestine and could have been considered Israeli. A significant portion of modern day Israel was [legally purchased](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_land_purchase_in_Palestine) off Arab inhabitants.
1.3.2.2.3.4.1. Con: In 1945, the land owned by Jewish peoples made up merely 7% of the combined area of [Israel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geography_of_Israel), the [West Bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Bank), and [Gaza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaza_Strip), with half owned by [the JNF and PICA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_land_purchase_in_Palestine#Land_purchases). It is hard to imagine how the remainder - nearly 19,000 square km - could have been legally purchased off Arab inhabitants.
1.3.2.2.3.4.2. Pro: The land wasn't considered stolen when it was under Jordanian and Egyptian control.
1.3.2.2.4. Con: If the right of the Palestinian people to self-determination clashes with the same right of the Jewish people, it is not clear how both can be respected.
1.3.2.3. Con: Palestinians seek recognition in order to access the right to self-determination. Yet self-determination is not a tangible element of international law \([p. 3](https://falklandstimeline.files.wordpress.com/2018/06/self-determination-daniel-thurer-thomas-burri.pdf)\). The US therefore makes no difference by refusing to recognise the state.
1.3.2.3.1. Pro: It is [not clear](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-determination#Defining_"peoples") how to define "peoples" and whether the Palestinians therefore have the right to self-determination.
1.3.2.3.1.1. Pro: In numerous cases the applicability of the right to self-determination is disputed, for example in the case of the [Kurdish people](http://opiniojuris.org/2017/09/28/kurds-right-self-determination-andor-secession/) and the [Basque people](https://www.reuters.com/article/us-spain-basque-self-determination/spains-basque-country-proclaims-right-to-self-determination-idUSKBN0E91R720140529).
1.3.2.3.1.2. Con: [Ethnic groups](https://www.britannica.com/topic/ethnic-group), even those spread across different countries, can still be easily defined as a "people."
1.3.2.3.2. Pro: Even where there is agreement on the existence of a people, it is not clear what the right to self-determination entails and how precisely it can be exercised \([p. 5](https://falklandstimeline.files.wordpress.com/2018/06/self-determination-daniel-thurer-thomas-burri.pdf)\).
1.3.2.3.3. Con: The principle of self-determination has [historical precedent](https://falklandstimeline.files.wordpress.com/2018/06/self-determination-daniel-thurer-thomas-burri.pdf)(The principle of self-determination was invoked on many occasions during World War II. It was also proclaimed in the → Atlantic Charter \(1941\) \(Declaration of Principles of 14 August 1941\), in which President Roosevelt of the United States and Prime Minister Churchill of the United Kingdom declared, inter alia, that they desired to see ‘no territorial changes that do not accord with the freely expressed wishes of the peoples concerned’ \(Principle 2 Atlantic Charter\), that they respected ‘the right of all peoples to choose the form of government under which they will live’ \(Principle 3 Atlantic Charter\) and that they wished to see ‘sovereign rights and self-government restored to those who have been forcibly deprived of them’ \(Principle 3 Atlantic Charter\)) and has been invoked on many occasions, dating back to WWII.
1.3.2.4. Con: If recognition by many other nations is sufficient, there is no reason why the US should be pressured into recognizing Palestine as a state.
1.3.2.4.1. Pro: Several other polities are partially recognised, however the US has not historically seen this as a compelling reason to recognise them itself.
1.3.2.4.1.1. Pro: Eighty-four UN states recognise the [Sahrawi Arab Democratic Republic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_recognition_of_the_Sahrawi_Arab_Democratic_Republic) \(Western Sahara\), however the US does not.
1.3.2.4.1.2. Pro: The Republic of China \(Taiwan\) is recognised by [19 UN member states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Taiwan#Entities_with_full_diplomatic_relations_with_Taiwan), as well as the Vatican, but not by the US.
1.3.2.4.1.3. Con: The US does not recognize the independence of Corsica and Catalonia against the wishes of, respectively, France and Spain.
1.3.2.4.1.3.1. Con: People in Corsica and Catalonia do not suffer to the same degree under the current situation as Palestinians do. That makes the comparison highly flawed.
1.3.2.4.1.4. Con: Kosovo is [recognized by the US](https://www.worldatlas.com/articles/how-many-countries-does-the-united-states-recognize.html) but not the UN; providing a precedent for recognizing certain countries without depending on the determinations from other countries.
1.3.2.4.2. Con: It may be possible to reduce the number of international law violations if the US recognises Palestine; this move creates political pressure.
1.3.2.4.2.1. Con: Israel has consistently [ignored](http://www.haaretz.com/study-israel-leads-in-ignoring-security-council-resolutions-1.31971) more UN Security Council decisions against it than any other country. It is unlikely to change its behaviour.
1.3.2.4.2.2. Con: The US has not historically conducted its geopolitical strategies according to international law. As such, reducing the number of international law violations through recognition of Palestine is unlikely to be a significant motivator for change.
1.3.2.4.2.2.1. Pro: The US invasion of Iraq was [illegal under international law](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2004/sep/16/iraq.iraq).
1.3.2.4.2.2.2. Pro: The US' continued use of drone strikes in undeclared war zones is illegal as per international law and [may even constitute war crimes](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2012/jun/21/drone-strikes-international-law-un).
1.3.2.4.2.2.3. Pro: Recognising Palestine could be viewed as a way for the US to divert attention away from more questionable parts of its foreign policy.
1.3.2.4.2.2.4. Con: Just because the US does not base its geopolitical strategy on emphasising international law does not mean it should not do so.
1.3.2.4.2.2.4.1. Pro: It is morally important for the US to do the right thing even if it does not have the best moral track record.
1.3.2.4.2.2.5. Con: International law and American support for it is actively denigrated if the US [openly supports](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2019/nov/18/us-israeli-settlements-no-longer-considered-illegal-palestinian-land-mike-pompeo) illegal policies for political gain.
1.3.2.4.2.2.5.1. Con: The US supports governments who regularly breach human rights, including [Egypt](https://www.theguardian.com/global-development/2021/jun/08/biden-accused-of-u-turn-over-egypts-human-rights-abuses), [Saudi Arabia](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-middle-east-55794286), and [the Philippines](https://www.openglobalrights.org/us-foreign-policy-aggravates-human-rights-abuses-in-the-philippines-during-covid/).
1.3.2.4.2.3. Pro: The [UN Fact Finding Mission](http://www2.ohchr.org/english/bodies/hrcouncil/docs/12session/A-HRC-12-48.pdf)(p. 284) in Gaza found that "some of the actions of the Government of Israel might justify a competent court finding that crimes against humanity have been committed," for example the [naval blockade](http://www.aljazeera.com/indepth/features/2011/10/20111030172356990380.html). If Palestine were to be established as a sovereign state, these crimes would receive more attention and condemnation from the international community.
1.3.2.4.2.3.1. Pro: The only way for the Palestinian people to escape their oppression is for them to achieve a semblance of political parity with Israel, which will only be achieved through international recognition.
1.3.2.4.2.3.2. Con: An earlier report by the UN, the [Palmer Report](https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/international-and-comparative-law-quarterly/article/abs/ii-the-palmer-report-and-the-legality-of-israels-naval-blockade-of-gaza/B02264DB75EBEF24B1C38547E5DBBE7C), declared the naval blockade on Gaza legal.
1.3.2.4.2.3.2.1. Con: The Palmer Report was intended to [mend relations](https://foreignpolicy.com/2011/09/02/u-n-s-gaza-flotilla-report-adrift-at-sea/) between Turkey and Israel rather than provide a factual assessment of the situation. Not only was the report more political than factual in nature, it also relied exclusively on information provided by Turkey and Israel, hence its conclusions cannot be considered definitive in either fact or law.
1.3.2.4.2.4. Con: America has already taken a stance [against](https://www.timesofisrael.com/despite-relentless-obama-criticism-settlements-grew-as-much-in-his-years-as-in-bushs/) settlements in the West Bank and, therefore, does not need to recognise Palestine as a state.
1.3.2.4.2.4.1. Con: The US has been [unable to stop](https://www.timesofisrael.com/despite-relentless-obama-criticism-settlements-grew-as-much-in-his-years-as-in-bushs/) Israel from expanding settlements. In fact, the rate of construction of such settlements has increased, so America does need to do more.
1.3.2.4.2.4.2. Con: Trump has given [mixed signals](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/feb/10/trump-apparent-u-turn-israeli-settlement-growth) about the construction of settlements in the West Bank. Recognising Palestine as a state will be a firm indicator that America does not tolerate further expansion of settlements on Palestinian territory.
1.3.2.4.2.5. Pro: Recognition of a Palestinian state is an important step in preventing Israeli de-facto [annexation](https://www.bbc.com/news/world-middle-east-52756427) of Jewish settlements in the West Bank.
1.3.2.4.2.5.1. Pro: 75% of Israelis would [not support](https://www.jpost.com/arab-israeli-conflict/half-of-israelis-support-annexation-25-percent-even-with-no-us-backing-poll-630129) annexation without USA support, and it is unlikely to support annexation after recognizing Palestine as a state.
1.3.3. Con: Recognising Palestine may jeopardise Israel's place in the region.
1.3.3.1. Pro: Israelis have a strong religious claim to Palestinian land; recognising Palestine would require giving up the land which is illegitimate.
1.3.3.1.1. Pro: Many holy texts, followed by more than a billion people, delegate Israel to the Jews.
1.3.3.1.1.1. Pro: Some Islamic scholars have noted that Palestinian claims to the land are not based on Quranic scriptures but on "popular demonization of the Jewish people." This can be interpreted to mean that, based on scriptural grounds, Israel should be delegated to the Jews.
1.3.3.1.1.1.1. Con: Muslims believe that Moses was Muslim, and therefore one could make an argument that the “Holy Land” mentioned in [Qur'an 5:21](https://quran.com/5/21) should now be under Muslim control.
1.3.3.1.1.2. Con: The number of followers a religion has tells us nothing about whether this religion's beliefs are more correct or relevant than any other religion's beliefs.
1.3.3.1.1.2.1. Pro: The claims in a religious text have no moral significance for ownership because the religions espoused within those texts are not true.
1.3.3.1.1.3. Con: The Torah recognizes that the land of Israel was the home for other older tribes before the Israelites arrived \([Pentateuch Shemot 34:11-12](https://www.chabad.org/library/bible_cdo/aid/9895/jewish/Chapter-34.htm)\). Thus, according to the Torah, the Israelites' descendants are not the most ancient inhabitants.
1.3.3.1.1.3.1. Con: Claims in the Torah are not consistent with prevailing academic opinion: the [Israelites](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israelites) were predominantly indigenous to Canaan.
1.3.3.1.1.4. Pro: In Judaism, [Jerusalem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jerusalem#Oldest_inscriptions) is the holiest city, and the Temple Mount at its center is the [holiest site](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holy_places#Judaism).
1.3.3.1.1.4.1. Con: Jerusalem is of great religious significance to Muslims too, and is considered to be the [third holiest site](http://www.pij.org/details.php?id=646) in Islam.
1.3.3.1.1.4.1.1. Pro: Jerusalem was the first [qibla](https://www.al-islam.org/the-message-ayatullah-jafar-subhani/chapter-29-change-qiblah), or direction of prayer, for Muslims, before the qibla became Mecca.
1.3.3.1.1.4.1.2. Pro: Jerusalem is the city from where Muhammad [ascended](https://www.bbc.co.uk/religion/religions/islam/holydays/lailatalmiraj.shtml) to Heaven and met Allah. It is an important religious event that is [celebrated](http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2014/05/27/lailat-al-miraj-2014_n_5395082.html) by Muslims every year throughout the world.
1.3.3.1.1.4.1.3. Con: Jerusalem and Zion are [mentioned](http://www.danielpipes.org/84/the-muslim-claim-to-jerusalem) almost a 1000 times in the Jewish scriptures but not once in the Quran.
1.3.3.1.1.4.1.3.1. Con: Even though the Quran does not mention Jerusalem itself, it [refers to](https://quran.com/17/1) the al-Aqsa Mosque in Jerusalem's old town.
1.3.3.1.1.4.1.3.2. Con: It is questionable whether references in holy scriptures are the appropriate standard to judge religious significance. The Bible does not mention the Vatican, the Pope or cardinals at any point, yet these stand at the center of the Catholic Church.
1.3.3.1.1.4.1.4. Con: Jerusalem has historically been used as a [political tool](http://www.danielpipes.org/84/the-muslim-claim-to-jerusalem) by Islamic empires. There is little religious importance affiliated with the city that outweighs Judaism's three thousand year old connection.
1.3.3.1.1.4.2. Pro: [Psalm 137](http://www.israelnationalnews.com/Articles/Article.aspx/11981) commands Jews to never forget about Jerusalem.
1.3.3.1.1.4.3. Con: Under the two state solution, Jews could [retain access](https://carnegieendowment.org/2018/09/18/two-states-or-one-reappraising-israeli-palestinian-impasse-pub-77269)(Full access to holy sites and freedom of worship will be granted to all religions, and the Old City within the walls will come under a special regime.) to their most important religious sites, such as the Temple Mount and the Wailing Wall in Jerusalem.
1.3.3.1.1.4.4. Pro: The religious significance of Jerusalem for Jews is the greatest of all three Abrahamic religion. Jews prayed toward [Al-Aqsa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mizrah#Jewish_law), Jerusalem before Islam and still pray toward it to this day in every single synagogue. Neither Christians nor Muslims pray toward Jerusalem or Al Aqsa.
1.3.3.1.1.5. Pro: Biblical claims are [very reliable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siloam_inscription#Translation) in [historical fact](http://blog.dovidgottlieb.com/search/label/Biblical%20archaeology) relevant to land claims.
1.3.3.1.1.5.1. Con: The original covenant that God made with Israel was broken when Israel chose to rebel against God. Consequently, God made [a new covenant](https://www.ucg.org/world-news-and-prophecy/palestine-israel-whose-land-is-it-really) with Israel, which represents all who believe in Him, regardless of ethnicity or heritage.
1.3.3.1.1.5.2. Pro: In [Genesis 12:7](http://biblehub.com/genesis/12-7.htm) and [15:18-21](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Gen%2015:18%E2%80%9321;&version=NIV;), God promised Greater Israel to the descendants of Abraham. Jews are the descendants of Abraham.
1.3.3.1.1.5.2.1. Pro: Textually, God explicitly limited this promise to the seed of Isaac \([Gen 21:10](https://www.chabad.org/library/bible_cdo/aid/8216#v=10) as endorsed by [21:12](https://www.chabad.org/library/bible_cdo/aid/8216#v=12) and [25:5](https://www.chabad.org/library/bible_cdo/aid/8220/jewish/Chapter-25.htm#v=5)\). This promise was then given to Jacob and his descendants \([Gen 28:4](https://www.chabad.org/library/bible_cdo/aid/8223#v=4)\).
1.3.3.1.1.5.3. Con: Holy texts [like the Bible](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historicity_of_the_Bible#Schools_of_archaeological_and_historical_thought) are not a good source to base modern land claims upon as they are notoriously unreliable and usually perceived to be not rooted in historic facts.
1.3.3.1.1.5.3.1. Con: Modern Zionists do not base their claim to the land of Israel on the Abrahamic covenant. Instead, the claim is based in part on [accepted history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arch_of_Titus) and [proven archeology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tel_Dan_Stele).
1.3.3.1.1.5.4. Pro: The Old Testament delineates the land of Israel in [Numbers 34:1-35:8](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Numbers%2034-35:8) and [Ezekiel 47:13-23](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Ezekiel%2047%3A13-23&version=NIV).
1.3.3.1.1.5.4.1. Con: This extract \([Numbers 34:1-35:8](https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Numbers%2034-35:8)\) says "Six of the towns you give the Levites will be cities of refuge, to which a person who has killed someone may flee," which is not respected nowadays, so what was once, no longer is.
1.3.3.1.2. Con: Only [14%](http://www.pcpsr.org/sites/default/files/Table%20of%20Findings_English%20Joint%20Poll%20Dec%202016_12Feb2017.pdf)(pg.14) of Israelis regard the claim to a "Greater Israel" - a [vision of Israel](https://www.jagranjosh.com/general-knowledge/what-is-greater-israel-why-is-the-idea-not-acceptable-to-the-muslim-community-1621507466-1) generated through a biblical and religious lens - as a priority.
1.3.3.1.3. Con: The United States is a [secular democracy](https://worldpopulationreview.com/country-rankings/secular-countries)(The United States is often considered to be "constitutionally secular." The U.S. Constitution states, "Congress shall make no law respecting an establishment of religion, or prohibiting the free exercise thereof." Additionally, keeping with the lack of an established state religion, Article Six of the U.S. Constitution states that "no religious Test shall ever be required as a Qualification to any Office or public Trust under the United States." The extent of separation between the U.S. government and religion continues to be debated. While the U.S. is a self-described secular state, there are still references to religion in the pledge of allegiance.) and should not take religious claims into consideration when deciding a matter of foreign policy.
1.3.3.1.3.1. Pro: Religious claims shouldn't determine the rightful rulers of a land. The Amerindian people have a [strong religion](http://nativeamericannetroots.net/diary/315) and [worship](http://indianrockpiles.weetu.com/articles/IndianRockPiles.html) specific places on the land of the United States of America and Canada, yet they are not its rulers. A similar situation exists in many other countries and regions.
1.3.3.1.3.1.1. Con: Due to their historical and religious claims on the regions in Canada and USA, Canadian Aboriginal have [self-governing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigenous_self-government_in_Canada#Self-government_agreements) territories and are actively promoting more autonomy from the federal government.
1.3.3.1.3.1.2. Con: Religious and historic claims should and do determine rightful ownership of the land. Many [court battles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Native_American_self-determination#Land_reclamation_and_anti-termination) have been won by natives to reclaim land which were taken by settlers.
1.3.3.1.3.1.2.1. Pro: [Blue Lake](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taos_Pueblo#Blue_Lake) was a sacred religious site for Amerindians and after many legal battles, U.S. has acknowledged the religious claim to the land and given back ownership to the Taos people.
1.3.3.1.3.2. Con: When making a decision that has an impact on a highly religious area of the world, it would be foolish to ignore religion entirely.
1.3.3.1.4. Con: Religious claims should not be seen as legitimate as Israel does enforce Jewish law.
1.3.3.1.4.1. Pro: Religious claims played [very little role](https://www.britannica.com/topic/Zionism)(A political turn was given to Zionism by Theodor Herzl, an Austrian journalist who regarded assimilation as most desirable but, in view of anti-Semitism, impossible to realize. Thus, he argued, if Jews were forced by external pressure to form a nation, they could lead a normal existence only through concentration in one territory. In 1897 Herzl convened the first Zionist Congress at Basel, Switzerland, which drew up the Basel program of the movement, stating that “Zionism strives to create for the Jewish people a home in Palestine secured by public law.”) in the early history of the Zionist movement and the creation of the state of Israel. Rather, the chief argument was the need to have a Jewish majority nation-state in order to enforce the rights of Jewish people.
1.3.3.1.4.2. Pro: Claims to benefits from adherence to a specific religion without living by the rules of that religion represents a form of cherry-picking. This is ethically problematic.
1.3.3.1.4.3. Con: Many religious sites are recognized as belonging to a specific country without having to prove their adherence to religious rules.
1.3.3.1.4.3.1. Pro: Strict interpretation of Shariah forbids women from working outside home as it is not their [natural state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Women%27s_rights_in_Saudi_Arabia#Employment). Yet, the international community does not question Saudi Arabia's control over Mecca and Medina even though Saudi women work outside their homes.
1.3.3.1.4.3.2. Pro: UNESCO has recognized the Dome of the Rock as Jordan's through a religious foundation \([p. 2](https://unesdoc.unesco.org/ark:/48223/pf0000246215)\). Nonetheless, [Jordanian law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Law_of_Jordan) is not religious.
1.3.3.2. Con: Jewish and Palestinian people have a shared strong historical claim to the land. It is illegitimate to prioritise one over the other.
1.3.3.2.1. Pro: Relying on history to judge the legitimacy of claims to land results in a regress to the original occupants. This would mean that peoples of most nations would have to surrender their land to those who were there before them which is infeasible.
1.3.3.2.1.1. Con: [A large number of](http://metrocosm.com/mapping-every-disputed-territory-in-the-world/) countries are disputed, and many have [independence](https://businessinsider.com.pl/international/experts-cant-figure-out-why-independence-movements-are-suddenly-sweeping-the-world/nssbjmk) movements. By recognising land claims, people are given more [sovereignty](https://www.oxfordreference.com/view/10.1093/oi/authority.20110803100520397) which affords choice - this is more important than feasibility.
1.3.3.2.1.2. Pro: If all people had to return to their native lands then [more than 98%](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States) of the American population would have to be sent back to the homelands of their ancestors. Migration on this scale would be highly disruptive.
1.3.3.2.2. Con: Jews are the oldest inhabitants of the region, with [ancestry](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0002929707613251) dating back 9500 years. Therefore, Palestinians cannot be considered to have an equal land claim.
1.3.3.2.2.1. Pro: The West Bank was part of Israel for a millennia, starting with the [Kingdom of Israel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_\(united_monarchy\)) in 1050 BCE, to the [Herodian Kingdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herodian_kingdom) 4 BCE. Jews led the first independent polity in the region.
1.3.3.2.2.1.1. Pro: Unlike the existence of an ancient Jewish sovereign over this land, there has never been a Palestinian state, nor, until recently, did Palestinians have a separate identity. They saw themselves as part of a larger Arab Syria \([p. 3](http://www.jewishvirtuallibrary.org/jsource/images/mf2017.pdf#page=43)\).
1.3.3.2.2.1.2. Con: By the same logic, the Egyptian people have an exclusive claim to much of the territory as they had incorporated it [into their empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt) by the 15th century BC.
1.3.3.2.2.1.3. Con: Even [during the period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Palestine#Independent_Canaanite.2C_Israelite_and_Philistine_period) with an Israelite presence in the region, other independent peoples were present. Thus, they should also have a claim to the land. Among them are the Philistines who [fought Israel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philistines) according to the Bible.
1.3.3.2.2.2. Con: Accounts of past Jewish sovereignty on the disputed territories are based on historical accounts not backed up by research and archaeological evidence.
1.3.3.2.2.2.1. Pro: The story of King David is not entirely supported by the scientific literature.
1.3.3.2.2.2.1.1. Con: The [Tel Dan Inscription](https://www.biblicalarchaeology.org/daily/biblical-artifacts/artifacts-and-the-bible/the-tel-dan-inscription-the-first-historical-evidence-of-the-king-david-bible-story/) is strong enough evidence for modern scholars to take the existence of King David seriously.
1.3.3.2.2.2.1.2. Con: King David’s palace is the best explanation for the discovery of the [Large Stone Structure](https://www.biblicalarchaeology.org/daily/biblical-sites-places/jerusalem/did-i-find-king-davids-palace/) discovered by Eilat Mazar.
1.3.3.2.2.2.1.3. Pro: There is a lack of [archaeological evidence](https://www.haaretz.com/archaeology/.premium-the-naked-truth-about-king-david-1.5324553) for the existence of King David.
1.3.3.2.2.2.1.4. Pro: The stories of who King David was and what he did are conflicting, and they give no [coherent account](https://www.haaretz.com/archaeology/.premium-the-naked-truth-about-king-david-1.5324553) of his life.
1.3.3.2.2.2.2. Pro: The historical evidence for the [Kingdom of Judah](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah#Archaeological_record) and the [Kingdom of Israel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_\(united_monarchy\)#Archaeological_record) is sparse and disputed; both were likely much smaller, less unified and less powerful than depicted in biblical accounts.
1.3.3.2.2.2.3. Pro: The biblical accounts of King Solomon and the empire he ruled have been [called into question](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solomon#Historicity). Little archaeological evidence has been found for the power he is usually ascribed or [for the temple](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solomon%27s_Temple#Archaeology) in Jerusalem named after him.
1.3.3.2.2.2.4. Con: While aspects of the story by which the Jewish people trace their ancient claim to sovereignty in Israel and Palestine are questionable, others are not. For example, the historical and archeological evidence of the [Hasmonean Dynasty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hasmonean_dynasty) which ruled that area just prior to its incorporation into the Roman empire, is clear.
1.3.3.2.2.2.5. Con: Archaeological studies support that Israelites were Canaanites who lived in a community which was called Israel, and [Israelite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israelites) culture and religion derived from Canaanite culture and religion. The name of god El and Yahveh derive from the supreme god Canaanite "El".
1.3.3.2.2.2.6. Con: Distinct Jewish culture is referenced in artifacts from 3000 years ago.
1.3.3.2.2.2.6.1. Pro: The Egyptian [Merneptah Stele](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Merneptah_Stele) confirms that Jews lived in Israel for more than 3000 years \(the stele was created in 1208 BC\).
1.3.3.2.2.2.6.2. Pro: The [Moab stele](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesha_Stele) \(from a bronze-age state in modern day Jordan\) confirms Jews lived in Israel 3000 years ago \(840 BC\).
1.3.3.2.2.2.6.3. Pro: The [Gezer calendar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gezer_calendar), discovered 20 miles west of Jerusalem, confirms the Canaanite origin of Jewish people. The tablet dates back to 1000 BC, a time when Hebrew and Canaanite culture had not yet diverged.
1.3.3.2.2.2.6.3.1. Con: There is some academic disagreement over the age of the Gezer calendar, which could disprove the cultural diversion timeline \([pp. 31-35](https://www.academia.edu/9206107)\).
1.3.3.2.2.2.6.4. Con: Artifacts "carrying our memories and traumas" experienced by the Palestinian people belong to the [Neolithic period](https://www.middleeasteye.net/discover/palestine-ancient-masks-dispossession)("For Palestinians, these historical objects are evocative. They act as vessels carrying our memories and traumas \(...\) these tactile forms are not merely material objects - they are tools that we identify with and resist with, and with which we exert our disappearing existence.\n\n"For Palestinians, they are emotional companions."). Thus, if artifacts are to serve as the basis for land ownership, Palestinians would have an advantage.
1.3.3.2.2.2.6.5. Con: It is unfair to base land ownership claims on artifacts because [Palestinian artifacts](https://www.972mag.com/archaeology-looting-israel-army/?fbclid=IwAR1l04GHipWrpLpkmqnGoXFP5lWNYm44OJPadUW3OaCjZIwnHKmEFGX6mYY)(Entitled “Finds Gone Astray,” the exhibition essentially glorified the Israeli military’s role in confiscating archaeological artifacts from the West Bank. According to the museum’s director, roughly 40,000 artifacts were “recovered” by the Israeli authorities.\n\nThis stark number of artifacts should raise major warning flags — especially during a time of increased black market activity, after the wars in Iraq and Syria. It is unclear where the artifacts were retrieved from, and not knowing their provenance makes it challenging to date them with credibility and prove their authenticity. This act of displacement gives museums, collectors, and cultural ministries the power to reframe theft as recovery and to loot houses as prestigious institutions.) have been seized or displaced by other countries, including Israel, in an effort to erase their identity.
1.3.3.2.2.3. Pro: Jews and Palestinians do not share a common heritage: Jews show [common ancestry](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1274378/#RF34) with other Neolithic Levantine and Iraqi populations, whereas Palestinians are of Arab origin.
1.3.3.2.2.3.1. Con: Genetically, Muslim Arabs in this region are Semitic people and [descend](https://www.haaretz.com/science-and-health/palestinians-and-jews-share-genetic-roots-1.5411201) from Abraham as much as the Jews; they have similar specific chromosomes and genes.
1.3.3.2.2.3.2. Con: The [HLA-DRB1](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5844529/) cluster [links](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5844529/)(Fig. 4) Palestinians with Ashkenazi and Sephardi Jews, making the three groups closer to each other than any other ethnic groups.
1.3.3.2.2.3.2.1. Con: The Levantine group, based on the HLA*-A and–B* cluster, shows that some Palestinians are equally close to some Ashkenazi, Lebanese, and Jordanian peoples \([Fig.5](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5844529/)\).
1.3.3.2.2.3.3. Pro: The [DQB1* 02:0X](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5844529/) allele is found in 20.9% of Palestinians' DNA, along with many Arab populations \(25.7% Bahraini, 35.9% Jordanian, 37.3% Saudi\). The HLA-[DRB1*11:01](https://journals.asm.org/doi/10.1128/CDLI.11.4.770-774.2004) allele is also a marker common for Arabs \(20.5% Palestinians, 26.6% Egyptians, 26.9% Lebanese\).
1.3.3.2.2.3.4. Pro: A [study](http://www.haaretz.com/1.5131928) has shown that 90% of Jews are [genetically](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israelites#Historical_Israelites) [linked](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5544389/#!po=62.0000) to the Levantine Canaanites. Canaanites lived in Jordan, Israel, and Lebanon, and were its first inhabitants. Jews are descendents of Canaanites living in Israel.
1.3.3.2.2.3.4.1. Con: Palestinians are [also](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5844529/) [linked](https://www.haaretz.com/science-and-health/palestinians-and-jews-share-genetic-roots-1.5411201) to the Levantine Canaanites. Jews, Palestinians, Druze, and Israeli Bedouins are genetically closer to each other than to any other population.
1.3.3.2.2.3.4.2. Pro: The different cultural groups who inhabited the Levant during the Bronze Age each had their own cultural identities, but shared a [common](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5544389/#!po=62.0000) genetics with Canaanites.
1.3.3.2.2.4. Pro: Muslims from the Arab Peninsula [invaded](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arab_conquest_of_the_Levant) Israel in 634 AD, and have, therefore, lived in the region for a shorter period of time.
1.3.3.2.2.5. Con: Palestinians have inhabited the region as long as Jews.
