Pro: It has been the consistent opinion of the [government of Israel](http://www.mfa.gov.il/mfa/foreignpolicy/peace/guide/pages/israel-%20the%20conflict%20and%20peace-%20answers%20to%20frequen.aspx#palestinianstate) that "a truly democratic Palestinian state fully at peace with Israel will promote the long-term security and well-being of Israel as a Jewish state."
1.5.4.3.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.1.6.2.
1.5.4.3.3. Con: An organised Palestinian security apparatus may still be antagonistic to Israel, given their history \([p. 40](https://csis-website-prod.s3.amazonaws.com/s3fs-public/legacy_files/files/media/csis/pubs/060209_palestforces.pdf)(The short-term prognosis is clearer. Whatever happens, relations\nbetween the Palestinians and Israel will now be more troubled than before. Creating effective Palestinian security forces capable of supporting the peace process will be more difficult, and both sides will show even less trust in the other.)\).
1.5.4.4. Con: Recognizing Palestine will jeopardize the security of Jewish settlers, as Palestine may wish to expel them and the protection offered by the IDF could be restricted.
1.5.4.4.1. Con: Israel [removed](http://www.nytimes.com/2005/08/18/world/middleeast/tearfully-but-forcefully-israel-removes-gaza-settlers.html?_r=0) Israeli settlers from Gaza when it withdrew in 2005. It can simply do the same now.
1.5.4.4.1.1. Con: -> See 1.1.4.3.5.5.3.1.
1.5.4.4.1.2. Pro: Withdrawal of Israeli settlers from occupied territories was regarded as a [strategic decision](https://reliefweb.int/report/israel/why-israel-pulling-out-settlers-gaza-west-bank) that best served Israel's national security. Given that this also aligns with Palestinian interests, such a move can be regarded as a win-win.
1.5.4.4.2. Con: -> See 1.4.3.3.2.
1.5.4.4.3. Con: Settlers are a minor contributor to Israeli opposition to a two-state solution. Surveys indicate that [only 20%](http://www.pcpsr.org/en/node/678) would change their opinion if settlers' security was guaranteed.
1.5.4.4.3.1. Con: That is still a significant portion of the population. Such a rise in support of a two-state solution has the potential of changing the calculus of domestic Israeli politicians.
1.5.4.4.4. Pro: Jewish settlements in the West Bank are [protected](http://www.haaretz.com/idf-soldiers-problem-in-west-bank-isn-t-palestinains-it-s-jews-1.401683) by the Israeli Defense Forces \(IDF\). US recognition could contribute to a partial withdrawal of this defense, thereby endangering these settlements.
1.5.4.5. Pro: Palestinian statehood lies at the center of Hamas' interests. Progress in this direction could lead to a more conciliatory attitude on their behalf.
1.5.4.5.1. Pro: Hamas [was founded](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hamas#Aims) as a resistance movement against Israel. Less-hostile relations between Israel and Palestine would undermine the raison d'être of Hamas.
1.5.4.5.1.1. Pro: Hamas' ability to militarily resist Israel is dependent on its ability to [operate amongst civilians](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2014/jul/24/gaza-hamas-fighters-military-bases-guerrilla-war-civilians-israel-idf) in the Gaza Strip. A peace deal may reduce public support for them, as there would no longer be a reason to advocate for militancy.
1.5.4.5.1.2. Con: Israel does not [consider](https://www.middleeastmonitor.com/20170223-hezbollah-and-hamas-are-too-weak-to-fight-says-israel-army-chief/) Hamas capable of putting up a fight at this time; therefore, the significance of reducing their raison d'être is limited.
1.5.4.5.1.3. Con: Without hostilities, both groups would cease to exist unless they pivot to another focal area or purpose.
1.5.4.5.1.4. Pro: Like Hamas, Hezbollah [was founded](http://www.bbc.com/news/world-middle-east-10814698) as an Islamic resistance movement against Israel and the United States. Improved Israel-Palestine relations would force them to revisit their objectives and demands.
1.5.4.5.1.4.1. Con: Hezbollah does little to threaten Israel or project Iranian power anymore. Its participation in the Syrian civil war has gotten it [stuck in a quagmire](https://www.brookings.edu/blog/markaz/2016/04/01/how-has-the-syrian-civil-war-affected-hezbollah-and-what-should-the-u-s-do/).
1.5.4.5.1.4.2. Pro: Hezbollah gained years of active [war experience](https://medium.com/war-is-boring/hezbollah-may-come-out-of-the-syrian-civil-war-stronger-than-ever-d1a72a05f3c4) while fighting the Syrian Civil War and has stockpiled a [huge number of weapons](https://jinsa.org/hezbollahs-missiles/), putting it in a strong position to fight Israel. It would be useful for Israel to placate the group.
1.5.4.5.1.4.3. Con: Hezbollah has stated their [disinterest](https://arabcenterdc.org/resource/hezbollah-is-not-interested-in-a-war-with-israel/) in engaging in warfare with Israel.
1.5.4.5.2. Pro: Hamas attacks Israel in order to achieve an independent, sovereign Palestine state. If this is achieved, attacks from Hamas will likely no longer be a concern.
1.5.4.5.2.1. Pro: Hamas [strategically](https://warontherocks.com/2014/07/going-at-it-in-gaza-new-realities-in-an-old-conflict/) attacks Israel when it runs out of money, in an attempt to force an agreement to relax its borders to allow finances to flow in. Recognition would mean lifting the blockade, eliminating the need for Hamas to attack Israel.
1.5.4.5.2.2. Con: Hamas may continue attacks against Israel as [its covenant](http://avalon.law.yale.edu/20th_century/hamas.asp) calls for the "obliteration" of Israel.
1.5.4.5.3. Con: Meeting Hamas' demands would likely make them less conciliatory. It's equally likely that they would claim their intransigence has paid dividends so far, and demand even more.
1.5.4.5.4. Pro: In the past, Hamas has been willing to negotiate and uphold agreements; the temporary [2008 ceasefire agreement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2008_Israel%E2%80%93Hamas_ceasefire) with Israel ended up breaking down after six months due to violations and disagreements on both sides.
1.5.4.5.5. Pro: In 2017, Hamas changed its core doctrine and [recognized](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/may/01/hamas-new-charter-palestine-israel-1967-borders) Israel's right to exist by accepting a Palestinian state with 1967 borders.
1.5.4.5.5.1. Con: Hamas recently appointed [Yahya Sinwar](https://www.nytimes.com/2017/02/13/world/middleeast/yehya-sinwar-hamas-gaza.html), a militant hard-liner, as its leader in Gaza. The new announcement by Hamas is not indicative of any moderation in its policies.
1.5.4.5.5.2. Con: The new Hamas announcement stated that "[We shall not waive an inch of the Palestinian home soil](https://www.aljazeera.com/news/2017/5/2/hamas-accepts-palestinian-state-with-1967-borders)." This suggests that, although, on paper, Hamas is willing to accept a two-state solution, in reality, it supports something entirely different.
1.5.4.5.5.3. Pro: The document outlining Hamas' revised stance was prepared with [goodwill](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/may/01/hamas-new-charter-palestine-israel-1967-borders)(The new document states the Islamist movement it is not seeking war with the Jewish people – only with Zionism that drives the occupation of Palestine.) and a desire to end the ongoing conflict with Israel and within the Palestinian movement.
1.5.4.5.5.3.1. Con: Revisions to Hamas' stance aimed to [de-link](https://www.usnews.com/news/world/articles/2017-05-01/hamas-to-soften-stance-on-israel-muslim-brotherhood-in-policy-document-sources) them from the Muslim Brotherhood to improve ties with Egypt and other Arab states, rather than to demonstrate genuine acceptance of Israel.
1.5.4.5.5.3.2. Pro: The document revising Hamas' stance was instrumental in demonstrating that the [objectives](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/may/01/hamas-new-charter-palestine-israel-1967-borders)(Hamas, which wrested control of the Gaza Strip in 2007 from the Ramallah-based Palestinian Authority – controlled by Fatah. Since then, all efforts since to reconcile the two Palestinian factions have faltered.\n\nThe new Hamas document essentially brings the two sides closer to the same negotiating objective.) of [Hamas and Fatah](https://www.aljazeera.com/features/2017/10/12/hamas-and-fatah-how-are-the-two-groups-different)(With the release of Hamas’ political document in 2017, the objectives of the two parties are effectively the same – creating a Palestinian state on the borders of 1967.), previously viewed as polarised, can be bridged.
1.5.4.5.5.3.2.1. Con: Hamas' [moderated stance](https://www.nytimes.com/2017/05/01/world/middleeast/hamas-fatah-palestinians-document.html?_r=0)(But the Hamas document, which has been leaking for weeks, is less a change in Hamas’s fundamental beliefs than a challenge for the credibility of Palestinians in both Gaza and the West Bank, as well as internationally.\n\n“Whether it’s a coincidence or it’s connected, I have one thing to say: The Palestinian leadership is afraid of this Hamas moderation,” said Mkhaimar Abusada, a political scientist at Al-Azhar University-Gaza. “Because the P.A. and Fatah are afraid that by this moderation, Hamas presents itself as the true representation of the Palestinian people,” he said, referring to the Palestinian Authority.) was an attempt to challenge Fatah over the leadership of the Palestinian people, rather than an acceptance of the two state solution.
1.5.4.5.5.3.2.2. Pro: In the months following the [publication of the charter](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/may/01/hamas-new-charter-palestine-israel-1967-borders), demonstrating a softer stance from Hamas, former rivals Hamas and Fatah signed a [reconciliation agreement](https://www.swp-berlin.org/publications/products/comments/2017C44_ass.pdf) \(p. 1\).

Hamas aggression will likely increase significantly if this were to take place.

The same would happen in the case that Israel ends it presence in Palestinian territory.
1.5.5.3.1. Pro: From June 2004 to July 2014, 26 Israeli civilians \(four of them minors\) and two foreign nationals were killed in Israel as a [result of rocket and mortar fire](http://www.btselem.org/israeli_civilians/qassam_missiles) by Hamas. This will continue, and may increase, if Israel withdraws.
1.5.5.3.2. Con: Hamas was [forced](http://www.slate.com/articles/news_and_politics/explainer/2012/11/hamas_in_gaza_how_the_organization_beat_fatah_and_took_control_of_the_gaza.html) to take over control of Gaza when President Mahmoud Abbas initially refused to surrender control of the government despite losing elections. This does not have to happen in the West Bank.
1.5.5.4. It is the land and the home of the Palestinian people. The expulsion and banishment of the Palestinian people from their land and the establishment of the Zionist entity therein do not annul the right of the Palestinian people to their entire land and do not entrench any rights therein for the usurping Zionist entity.) as opposed to the Palestinian Authority's support for an agreement based on the 1967 lines.
1.5.5.4.1. Pro: Hamas could take over the West Bank and East Jerusalem, preventing access to Jews, as [retaliation](http://www.aljazeera.com/news/2015/10/israel-limits-access-al-aqsa-compound-attacks-151009043215083.html) for recent Israeli restrictions on Palestinians visiting Al-Aqsa.

This undermines the assumption that Hamas seeks a harm beyond the removal of Israel.
1.5.5.4.2.1. Con: Most of the world's Jewish Population lives in [Israel](http://www.globalreligiousfutures.org/religions/jews)(Jews were largely concentrated in North America \(44%\) and the Middle East-North Africa region \(41%\) in 2010. Most of the remainder of the global Jewish population was found in Europe \(10%\) and the Latin America-Caribbean region \(3%\). In 2050, a majority of the world’s Jews \(51%\) are expected to live in the Middle East and North Africa \(mostly Israel\), while more than a third \(37%\) will live in North America. The share of the global Jewish population living in Europe is projected to decline to less than 8% by 2050.). If violence is used to remove Jews from the state in order to make it something other than a Jewish state, it is not unreasonable to think that this would constitute a Jewish genocide, regardless of the intent.
1.5.5.4.3. Pro: Many believe the slogan ["from the river to the sea"](https://www.jpost.com/diaspora/antisemitism/dutch-university-removes-from-the-river-to-the-sea-banner-after-protests-669847) to be a call for genocide.
1.5.5.4.4. Con: Whilst Hamas may have intended the chant in one manner, that is not how it is [interpreted](https://jewishcurrents.org/what-does-from-the-river-to-the-sea-really-mean/)(When Palestinians proclaim “from the river to the sea, Palestine will be free,” many Zionists argue that this is a Palestinian call for genocide. But as historian Maha Nassar has noted, there has never been an “official Palestinian position calling for the forced removal of Jews from Palestine.” The links between this phrase and eliminationism might be the product of “an Israeli media campaign following the 1967 war that claimed Palestinians wished to ‘throw Jews into the sea.'” Jewish groups such as the American Jewish Committee also claim that the slogan is antisemitic because it has been taken up by militant groups such as the Palestine Liberation Organization, the Popular Front for the Liberation of Palestine, and Hamas. But as Nassar writes, the phrase predates these uses, and has its origins as “part of a larger call to see a secular democratic state established in all of historic Palestine.”) today by those who sing it.
1.5.5.5. This entails making your opponent believe you are willing to take unrealistic measures \(such as a war of annihilation\) in order to win, despite this being a bluff.

1.6. Con: Palestinians refuse to recognise Israel as a Jewish state; it is, therefore, hypocritical of them to demand that other actors recognise them as a legitimate state.
1.6.1. Con: Palestinians may not want to recognise Israel as Jewish in order to protect the legitimacy of their claims of religious access to Jerusalem.
1.6.1.1. Pro: Recognition that Israel is Jewish could legitimise Jewish demands for access to the [Temple Mount](https://www.pri.org/stories/2012-10-22/long-running-jerusalem-dispute-heats-again), which would violate Islamic religious instruction.
1.6.1.2. Pro: If Israel is recognised as Jewish, then all religious sites within Israel are likely to be recognised as primarily Jewish. This provides a justification for restricting the ability of Muslims to access these sites.
1.6.1.2.1. Pro: UNESCO has [criticised](https://www.theatlantic.com/news/archive/2016/10/unesco-controversy-over-jerusalems-holy-sites/504149/) current Israeli policy of restricting access to religious sites for Muslims in Jerusalem. It will only likely further decrease access after recognition of Israel as Jewish.

1.6.1.2.2. Con: Jews are forbidden from praying at the [Temple Mount](https://www.newyorker.com/news/news-desk/furor-temple-mount), even though it is located in Jerusalem.
1.6.1.3. Con: Recognition of a Jewish state doesn't necessarily imply that religious sites within Israel are primarily Jewish.
1.6.2. Con: Accepting the Jewishness of Israel would mean giving up the '[right of return](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestinian_right_of_return)' for the Palestinian refugees who left during the 1948 exodus.
1.6.2.1. Con: The "official" refugee community number is in the millions only because the surrounding [Arab states](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/middle-east/no-way-home-the-tragedy-of-the-palestinian-diaspora-1806790.html) refuse to grant citizenship to the millions of children and grandchildren born within their borders.
1.6.2.2. Con: The notion of a Palestinian 'right to return' is problematic.
1.6.2.2.1. Pro: Various groups of people would be entitled to a 'right to return' similar to that requested by Palestinians. Yet, they either don't claim it or don't have it recognized. It would be illegitimate to acknowledge Palestinian claims because of this.
1.6.2.2.1.1. Pro: [Millions of Germans in Eastern Europe](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flight_and_expulsion_of_Germans_\(1944–1950\)) were expelled to Germany after World War II, but their explusion does not entitle them to a right of return.
1.6.2.2.1.2. Pro: [Hundreds of thousands of Jews](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jewish_exodus_from_Arab_and_Muslim_countries) were [expelled](http://www.timesofisrael.com/jewish-refugees-from-arab-lands-seek-justice-at-united-nations/) from Arab nations and many found a new home in Israel. These refugees have not been given a right of return to their homelands by Arab states, so it is hypocritical to demand that for Palestinian refugees.
1.6.2.2.1.3. Pro: Many [ethnic Greeks and Turkish peoples](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Greece_and_Turkey) could demand a right to return similar to the Palestinians. Yet, they do not.
1.6.2.2.2. Pro: The 'right to return' [does not exist](https://link.springer.com/chapter/10.1007/978-3-540-68161-8_7) in contemporary international law as Palestinians would wish it to be recognised.
1.6.2.2.2.1. Pro: Being a victim of the injustice of expulsion does not necessarily grant the right to resettle in native lands.
1.6.2.3. Pro: Up to [8 million](https://www.gatestoneinstitute.org/4184/palestinian-refugees-return) Palestinian refugees wish to return to Israel, and strongly condemned Mahmoud Abbas's suggestion of giving up their right to return.
1.6.2.4. Pro: If this right to return is given up, up to 450,000 Palestinian refugees would be [forced](https://www.gatestoneinstitute.org/3770/lebanon-apartheid-laws) to continue to live in inhumane conditions in Lebanese refugee camps.
1.6.2.5. Con: Palestinian refugees do not have a clear right of return to Israel \([p. 1](http://arizonajournal.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/11/Kramernote.pdf)(Meanwhile, the Israelis assert that Palestinians do not have a right of return under international law, or if there is a right, it does not apply to the case of the Palestinian refugees. Moreover, the various international law sources that purport to embody a Palestinian right of return are often ambiguous and open to different interpretations.)\).
1.6.2.5.1. Pro: [Jewish leadership](http://www.slate.com/articles/news_and_politics/foreigners/2011/11/israel_and_1948_did_israel_plan_to_expel_its_arabs_in_1948_or_not_.html) did not plan to expel Arabs from the state-to-be of Israel; rather, that was the consequence of outbreak of violence between Arabs and Jews. As a result, the state of Israel bears no responsibility for Palestinians refugees.
1.6.2.5.1.1. Con: Some believe that the 1948 Palestinian exodus was the result of a planned ethnic cleansing implemented by Zionist leaders through systematic expulsions of villages and terrorist attacks against civilians \([p. 15-16](https://yplus.ps/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/Pappe-Ilan-The-Ethnic-Cleansing-of-Palestine.pdf)(‘The military campaign against the Arabs, including the “conquest and destruction of the rural areas” was set forth in the Hagana’s Plan Dalet’.5 The aim of the plan was in fact the destruction of both the\nrural and urban areas of Palestine.\nAs the first chapters of this book will attempt to show, this plan was both the inevitable product of the Zionist ideological impulse to have an exclusively Jewish presence in Palestine, and a response to developments on the ground once the British cabinet had decided to end the mandate. Clashes with local Palestinian militias provided the perfect context and pretext for implementing the ideological vision of an ethnically cleansed Palestine. The Zionist policy was first based on retaliation against Palestinian attacks in February 1947, and it transformed into an initiative to ethnically cleanse the country as a whole in March 1948.)\).
1.6.2.5.1.2. Pro: The refugee crisis was caused by the Arab [invasion of Israel](https://www.adl.org/resources/glossary-terms/palestinian-refugees) in 1948. Therefore, the respective Arab states are responsible for solving the crisis.
1.6.2.5.1.3. Pro: Many of the Palestinian Arabs who fled during the Arab invasion in 1948 did so [voluntarily](https://www.adl.org/education/resources/glossary-terms/palestinian-refugees) to avoid the ongoing war or at the urging of Arab leaders who promised that all who left would return after a quick Arab victory over the new Jewish state.

1.6.3.1. Pro: Recognizing a state as solely belonging to a religious or ethnic group is exclusionary and unnecessary.

1.6.3.1.4.1.4.1. Pro: The demand to recognize Israel as a Jewish state is a [delaying tactic](http://foreignpolicy.com/2011/05/25/should-the-palestinians-recognize-israel-as-a-jewish-state/) used by Israel.
1.6.3.1.4.1.4.1.1. Con: Most countries [delay their recognition](https://academic.oup.com/fpa/article/14/3/367/2625552)(A large number of states, including many located in the global south, can be found in the middle ground, seeking to balance and mantle their positions and often delaying the decision to recognize Kosovo.) of other fledgling states in order to balance their arguments and come up with a decision.
1.6.3.1.4.1.4.1.2. Pro: There is [evidence](http://www.haaretz.com/israel-news/.premium-1.569459) that it was invented by Netanyahu to stymie peace processes with the Palestinians.
1.6.3.1.4.1.4.2. Con: The Zionist program, adopted at the [First Zionist Congress in Basel in 1897](http://www.jewishvirtuallibrary.org/first-zionist-congress-and-basel-program-1897), specified that the “aim of Zionism is to create for the Jewish people a home in Palestine, secured by public law.” This indicates that it is not feasible to separate Israel from its Jewish nature.
1.6.3.1.4.1.4.2.1. Pro: [The Jewish State](http://www.jewishvirtuallibrary.org/quot-the-jewish-state-quot-theodor-herzl), written by Theodor Herzl, is considered to be the foundational text of the Zionist movement and the creation of Israel. Thus, from the beginning, Israel was conceptualised as a Jewish state and should, therefore, be recognised as such.
1.6.3.1.4.1.4.3. 1.6.3.1.4.1.4.4. Con: [Israel's Declaration of Independence](http://www.mfa.gov.il/mfa/foreignpolicy/peace/guide/pages/declaration%20of%20establishment%20of%20state%20of%20israel.aspx) states that Israel is a Jewish state, making it clear that this is a key aspect of the state's foundational identity.
1.6.3.1.4.2. Pro: In countries like [Yemen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religion_in_Yemen), majority of the citizens are Muslim; yet, they do not call for international recognition as an Islamic state.

1.6.3.2. Pro: Palestinians are asking only for recognition of Palestine as a state, not as a Muslim state. Therefore, their refusal to recognise Israel as a Jewish state is not hypocritical.
1.6.4. Pro: Recognizing Israel would directly confer strategic benefits on Palestine while also indirectly dispelling any accusations of hypocrisy from the internal community.
1.6.4.1. Con: Israel uses its claim as a Jewish state to [block](https://www.thenational.ae/the-loaded-terms-israel-uses-to-justify-its-inflexible-stance-1.334411) a peace deal with Palestinians.

Pro: Israel is a [de jure state](https://www.jewishvirtuallibrary.org/united-states-grants-de-jure-recognition-to-israel), and [Palestine depends](https://www.washingtoninstitute.org/policy-analysis/palestinian-economic-dependence-israel) on Israel at the moment; it would, therefore, be counterintuitive for them to not recognize Israel.
1.6.4.3. Pro: By refusing to recognize Israel's right to exist, Palestine positions itself in opposition to many of its \(potential\) international supporters. 1.6.4.3.1. Pro: Palestine opposing Israel's right to exist puts Germany in a problematic position vis-à-vis its support for Palestine. 1.6.4.3.2. Pro: Opposing Israel's right to exist complicates relations with the US, whose presidents, such as [Barack Obama](https://www.nbcnews.com/news/world/obama-stresses-safety-israelis-self-determination-palestinians-flna1c9000752)(And the president used a Hebrew phrased to express the overwhelming theme of the speech: “Ah-tem lo lah-vahd.” This means, “You \[Israel\] are not alone.”), have extended their support to Israel.
1.6.4.3.3. Con: The right to exist is a concept alien to [international law](https://theconversation.com/israel-has-no-right-to-exist-and-neither-does-any-other-state-1668)(The problem with such a demand is that no such abstract “right to exist” can be found in international law or in any serious theory of international relations.\n\nTo put it succinctly, a “right to exist” does not exist for states. Nor does such a right exist in practice.), therefore Palestine cannot be punished through legal means.
1.6.4.4. Con: Asking Palestinians to accept Israel's right to exist equals asking Palestinians [to accept as justified](https://www.csmonitor.com/2007/0202/p09s02-coop.html) what has been done to them over the course of the past decades.
1.6.4.4.1. Con: Accepting Israel's right to exist as a Jewish state does not constitute accepting that all Israeli actions are acceptable.
1.6.4.4.1.1. Pro: Accepting Israel's right to exist as a Jewish state could be seen as a way to acknowledge the reality and legitimacy of Israel's existence and as desire for a future where both nations can exist in peace.
1.6.4.4.1.2. Con: Israel [uses](https://www.thenational.ae/the-loaded-terms-israel-uses-to-justify-its-inflexible-stance-1.334411) its claim to the land of Palestine to normalise settlements on Palestinian territory and legitimise unjust actions. Acknowledging Israel as a Jewish state would [entail](https://www.aljazeera.com/opinions/2011/9/30/why-israel-cant-be-a-jewish-state/) accepting this.
1.6.4.4.2. Pro: For many Palestinians, the existence of Israel is necessarily predicated on the [1948 expulsion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1948_Palestinian_exodus) of hundreds of thousands of Palestinians.

1.6.4.4.4. Con: Much of the harmful actions that the Israeli state has taken against Palestinians have been justified by citing [general security concerns](https://www.hrw.org/news/2017/06/04/israel-50-years-occupation-abuses), not by citing its claims to the land.
1.6.4.4.4.1. Pro: If recognizing the Jewish state acknowledges or affirms Israeli atrocities or injustices committed against Palestinians, then recognizing the Palestinian state would affirm Palestinian atrocities and injustices committed against Israel.
1.6.4.4.4.1.1. Con: Israel can be charged for state-sponsored crimes. However, this is not the case with Palestine, which is currently not recognized as a state.
1.6.4.4.4.1.1.1. Pro: The Palestinian Authority has [control only over a part of the land](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestinian_National_Authority#/media/File:Zones_A_and_B_in_the_occupied_palestinian_territories.svg), and [Israel already punishes](http://www.aljazeera.com/indepth/features/2017/01/israel-collective-punishment-jerusalem-attack-170110084017716.html) those even suspected of violence and their families in Israel, in the West Bank, and Gaza.
1.6.4.4.4.1.1.2. Pro: No external authority has ever held Israel accountable for its state-sponsored crimes.
1.6.4.4.4.2. Con: Resistance to military occupation is a [humanitarian right](https://ihl-databases.icrc.org/applic/ihl/ihl.nsf/b0d5f4c1f4b8102041256739003e6366/6c86520d7efad527c12563cd0051d63c), not a crime.
1.6.4.4.4.2.1. Con: The Geneva convention does not grant anybody the right to attack civilians or put civilians in danger through illegal actions. A war crime is a war crime.
1.6.4.4.4.2.2. Con: This claim falsely assumes that resistance to military occupation is all that Palestians are guilty of in relation to atrocities committed against Israel.
1.6.4.4.4.2.2.1. Pro: In the war of 1948, Palestinian Arabs openly and proudly sought the [total annihilation](http://www.jewishvirtuallibrary.org/background-and-overview-israel-war-of-independence) of the Jews.
1.6.4.4.4.3. Con: Violence as a means of alleviating personally-perceived fear is not a legitimate argument.
1.6.4.4.5. Pro: Recognition by many other nations is a sufficient condition for a state to be legitimate. Therefore, Palestinian recognition of Israel has no positive effects on Israel, but strengthens Israel's position in relation to already occupied land, its general approach to the Palestinian people, and potential war crimes.
1.6.5. Con: Other states have not recognised Israel as a Jewish state, or been asked to, yet the US does not refuse to recognise those states.
1.6.5.1. Con: Most other states are not proximate to Israel or heavily involved with Israel geopolitically, so whether they recognise Israel or not is mostly irrelevant. The same is not true for Palestine.
1.6.5.2. Con: It would be difficult for the US to withdraw its recognition of established states. In contrast to that, it is easy to reject the recognition of a disputed state.
1.6.5.3. Pro: Several major American allies, such as Pakistan and Saudi Arabia, as well as states that receive large amounts of US support, such as Afghanistan and Iraq, [do not recognize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_recognition_of_Israel) Israel either.
1.6.5.4. Pro: The demand for recognition as a Jewish state was not included in the treaties with [Jordan](http://www.kinghussein.gov.jo/peacetreaty.html) or [Egypt](http://www.mfa.gov.eg/Lists/Treaties%20DB/Attachments/645/Peace%20Treaty_en.pdf), though - like Palestine - they exist in close proximity to Israel and have histories of antagonism.
1.6.5.4.1. Pro: Jordan and Israel have [some strong economic incentives](https://www.ynetnews.com/articles/0,7340,L-4364003,00.html)(First-of-its-kind cooperation\n\nA similar way is made by goods imported from Turkey and neighboring countries to Jordan and Iraq, which arrive at the port on ships. The vessels unload their cargo there, and the merchandise is taken by trucks to the Jordan River Crossing on its way to Jordan and Iraq.\n\nAccording to estimates, the goods transported through Israel are worth tens of millions of dollars a month.) to put aside their differences; international recognition of a Jewish state is not a priority for Israel.
1.6.5.4.2. Pro: The Egypt-Israel peace accord was [signed in 1979](https://web.archive.org/web/20110903011255/http://www.mfa.gov.il/MFA/Peace%20Process/Guide%20to%20the%20Peace%20Process/Camp%20David%20Accords); recognition of a Jewish state is a demand that came much later.
1.6.5.4.3. Con: Jordan and Egypt are [more powerful](https://arabcenterdc.org/resource/egypt-iraq-jordan-alliance-tries-to-change-middle-east-dynamics/) regional players than Palestine. Israel likely opted against demanding the pair to recognize Israel as a Jewish state in order to remain on good terms with them.
1.6.5.5. Pro: The Palestinian refusal to recognize Israel as a Jewish state is backed by the [Arab League](http://www.haaretz.com/middle-east-news/1.578785).
1.6.5.6. Con: All nations have rivals, and some may choose not to recognize another over a political spat; one country refusing to recognize Palestine does not justify others following suit.
1.6.5.6.1. Pro: [Armenia and Azerbaijan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armenia%E2%80%93Azerbaijan_relations) do not recognize each other due to border conflicts; [Morocco and Algeria](https://www.france24.com/en/africa/20210826-explainer-why-did-algeria-cut-diplomatic-ties-with-morocco) have had political spats leading them to cut diplomatic ties with one another. Neither case is cause for the rest of the world to stop recognizing any of the countries involved.
1.6.5.7. Con: Most other states have not rejected the right of Israel to exist, even if they do not recognise it as Jewish. The context of their relationship with Israel is thus different.
1.6.5.7.1. Con: Iranian leaders have rejected Israel's right to exist [on numerous occasions](https://www.theatlantic.com/international/archive/2015/03/Iranian-View-of-Israel/387085/), going so far as to describe it as Iran's mission to erase Israel from the map.
1.6.6. Pro: PLO chief Mahmoud Abbas has repeatedly pledged that he will [not recognize Israel](http://www.timesofisrael.com/in-fiery-speech-abbas-rejects-jerusalem-concessions/)(“I say, listen, listen, the Palestinian people won’t kneel, and we tell the world, listen, listen the Palestinian people won’t kneel,” he said at one point, drawing chants of “Abu Mazen, Abu Mazen.”\n\n“Without east Jerusalem as a capital of the state of Palestine, there will be no peace between us and Israel,” Abbas said.\n\nAbbas also reiterated that he will not recognize Israel as the homeland of the Jewish people. Netanyahu has raised such a demand, and there is growing expectation that it will be included in Kerry’s proposal.\n\n“We will not recognize it,” Abbas said. “We will not accept and it’s our right not to recognize the Jewish state.”) as a Jewish state, meaning he will never recognize Israel in full.
1.6.6.1. Pro: The [PLO's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greater_Palestine) [true aim](https://www.ajc.org/translatehate/From-the-River-to-the-Sea) is to subsume all of mandatory Palestine into a Palestinian state.
1.6.6.1.1. Pro: PLO's [emblem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palestine_Liberation_Organization) suggests that the desired state is the entirety of [Israel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Palestine).
1.6.7. Pro: One of the two former Prime Ministers of the Palestinian National Authority, Ismail Haniyeh, [pledged](http://www.timesofisrael.com/hamas-official-denies-group-could-recognize-israel/) that he will not recognise Israel.
